2016-03-22
Battle Line is relatively simple if you are familiar with hands of Poker. Although luck plays a role when drawing cards, at least it feels like that you are in control, mostly. The tactical element is the most prominent feature when you are choosing which card to play to win a battle. You are also forced to play your hand which is kind of crisis management or maximisation of potential depending on which way you look at it. Longer term strategy is needed only when deciding which battles you are trying to win.

I enjoy Battle Line although I cannot find anything very special about it.

What's funny about this is that although Knizia's infamous for pasting theme on to his games, the theme almost seems to work here.  Not so much in the cards and so forth -- their art is nice enough, but in the overall development.  I find myself silently fitting a combat narrative to the action.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.1
A quick playing two-player rummy style game, but with very interesting decisions.  Effectively trying to establish strong positions on a flag (and preserving the ability--probability wise--to maximize the power of a position given the limited card hand size), while not tipping your hand to your opponent so they can never be comfortable that you are in a beatable position.

At the same time, realizing that you will likely have to sacrifice one or two flags rather than reduce the power of your other positions, and making sure those flags do not allow an opponent to achieve a break-through.  

There's also a bit of gamesmanship in when and how tactics cards are employed, given the rule limiting the ability of player to play a number of tactics cards that is no greater than one more than the number of tactics cards played by the opponent.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9

I would have given this a 10 rating, though I do have one wish, and that's that the artwork was better. It's not bad artwork, it's just not as stellar as the game itself is. So, wishing the artwork was better, I decided to knock this down to a 9.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I have played a lot of games of hand management, but this rules supreme.  It is one of my favorite Euros
.

You are playing 9 simultaneous hands of poker-lite face up with 6 "suits" (colors).  If you win 3 hands in a row or 5 of 9, you win.  Each turn, you draw a card and MUST play one. Therein lies the difficultly: you don't want to get rid of cards.  You want to hold them and see what you can play out, but instead, you are flying by the seat of your pants and constantly improvising.

It's money-less poker that manages to keep you engaged.  This simple little game requires as much skill in hand management as any that I have seen.  The theme is really weak, though: it's just NOT the game that you are going to scream, "SPARTA!!!!!" in, ya know?

But despite that shortcoming, this is by far the best Euro/Knizia game that I have ever played.

RATING: 4
This game looks awful. The graphic design comes across as cheap rather than sparse. It evokes Solitaire, which correlates to boredom and waste in my mind.

The game itself is straightforward. For mine, like a lot of Knizias, too straightforward. Like Lost Cities, which is only marginally worse, there isn't that much going on. You manage your hand to form the best combinations within the broader diminishing probabilities of the overall set. This is the backbone of many competitive card games, but this game breaks down because there just aren't that many useful combinations. I think you really have to play for trips and straight flushes; the odds just aren't there on the other combinations. 

Without tactics, this is a very dry game of probabilities. With tactics, it's a weak game of probabilities that can be jolted into irrelevance by swingy effects. I will concede there is some tension around key positions occasionally, but I think if you are focused on accurately assessing your position you will find there is precious little control over the outcomes beyond your immediate hand. 

I'D RATHER WE PLAYED:[thing=43022][/thing]. Two-player card games, in particular, need not only a valuation component, but a hand-reading component. Battle Line is no more than Solitaire in any meaningful way because you can't make reasonable predictions of your opponent's hand, and thus future plans. Without this, the hidden information of card games is just variance.

RATING: 7.5
Battle Line is poker-like (and I hate and despise poker), but the tactical cards add a delicious element to the game that poker just doesn't have. Really, the pasted-on theme is irrelevant to the game.....and yet, for some strange reason, I can't imagine myself enjoying it as much with just about any other theme. Many people have compared it with Balloon Cup and Lost Cities; for myself, it reminds me of a much shorter and much simplified version of Twilight Struggle, without Defcon! Of course, Battle Line (and its Scottish predecessor) far predate Twilight Struggle: did Messrs Gupta and Matthews have visions of hoplites dancing through their heads when designing TS???????

RATING: 9
2

Can play with or without tactics (and, really, you can almost have a game without tactics cards simply by abstaining). It's extremely tense, and there are often agonizing decisions about when to "commit" to a hand (as in, if you commit to a flush you're ceding to everything better than that) and when to abandon a pawn. You have to think through when a pawn can be claimed (though this doesn't often take too long), and it all plays quickly. It's quick, fun, tense, and thinky. Though abstract, it feels like it plays differently. There is a substantial luck element, but it doesn't kill the fun.

RATING: 9.5
The perfect two player game. Spent ages trying to track down a copy (did not want the silly Scottish version) defnitely worth it.

We play that when you play the Scout card you can only draw 2 tactics cards and one from the deck. Also, we play that one player cannot play both Alexander and Darius. If a player draws both then they can swap Darius for a non-tactic card (the Darius card being discarded from the game).
These two variants avoid one player having too great of an advantage due to lucky drawing of tactics cards.

RATING: 8
RK, the living legend of board game design, makes another hit.  Although simple, it is definitely innovative and spunky.  The use of poker hands to prove that you have one flag or another, over a nice solider formation theme, is a nice light touch.  Its far from any kind of simulation, but it is very addicting and games are very quick.  The flaw is when it tries think it is more advanced then it really is, with the option to draw from a tactics deck, which sort of just gives you cards that allow you to win a pile, rather than really altering gameplay, and it is too random to develop any strategies for.  All in all, still a clever little card game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
My dislike for this game probably goes lower than the '5' rating, but my wife did like it, and I want to be fair because there is something here.  I'd play it again, but it would be hard to take it at all seriously.

Ultimately, the problem is that the game is too heavily influenced by luck.  It's deceptive at first because there are a lot of decisions.  You have to manage your hand, choose your formations, choose the best spots to play them, track your opponent, count cards, and play the odds.  

The tactics cards add a much needed deeper level of possibilities.  (Though since you can only play one more tactic card than your opponent has, these cards can be frozen out of play rather easily.)

But in the end, between two competent players, the game is just too easy to play (nearly) optimally.  And then it just comes down to who draws more of the cards they need -- and who doesn't.  After all the hand management, you and your opponent will settle down on a handful of formations that require one or a few cards to complete and you'll take turns drawing to see who gets it.  

It's very unsatisfying.  For something this light, I'd much rather play Lost Cities.

RATING: 7
A reworking of Schotten Totten but with a more cut throat feeling.  For a card game this does a very good job of simulating an ancient battle of 2 forces.  There are weak points and strengths along the line, but like a real battle, these can sometimes prove to be illusions.  The game plays fast with a element of deduction as the cards are played out and options are created or removed based on card plays.  A good player will keep an eye on the cards and know when to claim a victory before an opponent can disrupt the battle with a command card.  Best played as the best of 3 games since the games are so short.  One of Knizia's solid designs.  [GTI: AGCL]

RATING: 7
Battle Line is a stereotypical "couple's game" that I continue to play on a regular basis. Part of the reason is the simplicity, quickness, and familiarity... but the game itself hasn't annoyed me like other gateway games have. So many lighter games seem to fall prey to easy-decisions and winners/losers being determined by dumb luck. While battle line isn't a deep game, there are always meaningful decisions in each game. 

Today, I have grown a bit tired of it because I've played this game so much (much more than my recorded plays). Yet, it keeps getting played and I keep enjoying each game. This game is a keeper.

RATING: 8
Two-player Knizia card game, with the player playing three card poker hands over seven 'flags', trying to win either five in total or three in a row.

I like this, and I like better than Knizia's other two player card game Lost Cities (which I still like) as Battle Line got a bit more strategy to it than Lost Cities. The components are okay (better than I expected from seeing reviews, but not as good as Lost Cities), but its complete colourblindness-unfriendliness is a deal breaker for me (my current two-player opponent is colourblind). Sigh.

RATING: 10
This really is a perfect little 2-player game.  It has quite simple rules, especially if you print out a player aid to remind you of the precedence of formations.  What I really like about it is how quickly the decision of what card to play becomes challenging.  With a bad opening draw, it can happen right away, and it only gets harder as you have to count the cards that are out there and do some reasoning.  I should say that I think the game is deeper and more fun when you play with the "Advanced rule" that you can only claim flags at the start of your turn.  Rarely can you do anything productive in these situations, but having that possibility is key, plus being able to slough even one card in these situations helps to make you feel you are still in the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Great for a quick two player game.  You compete for different "flags" in the battle line by trying to make the best poker hand with three cards.  Whoever has the better hand takes that flag.  There is also a special deck with cards that allow unique actions (like moving an opponent's card to your side of the battle line).  This really keeps things interesting, because victory can be snatched from you in unexpected ways.

RATING: 9
This is an amazing two player card game with a theme that I feel matches the mechanics well. You are basically making poker hand to win flags in a battle, but there is a deep level of play to this game that will surprise you. Play without the tactic cards however, as they make the game chaotic and also use the point system to play at least two games per match. this minimizes the effect of luck. A skilled player will beat a lesser skilled player consistently, as I have proven with numerous opponents!There is a lot of calculations that go on during a match, much more than Lost Cities for example. For competitive play a chess clock is a good idea, as one can spend a lot of time trying to figure out the most promising card play. Early versions from GMT had thin cardstock, newer versions have sturdier stock and is much better.You can play a slightly simpler version called Schotten-Totten here: www.flexgames.com

I've lowered the rating from a 10 because I now believe that whomever gets dealt the most 10-9-8 straight flushes tends to win among skilled opponents.

RATING: 10
I'm even teaching my father-in-law the importance of the 'order filler' game.  Had Battle Line on my Christmas list along with a variety of other games.  Gave my in-laws the Thoughthammer website and told them of the free shipping deal.  They were planning on only getting me two games, but fell a little short, so they added Battle Line to the order to get the free shipping.  I'm so glad they did.  What a neat little game.  The tension is immense for what on the surface may seem to be just 3-card poker.  It is so much more, however.  Everyone I've played with wants to immediately play another game after the first one. The tactics cards can either be a blessing or a curse, and card play necessitates tipping your hand as little as possible.  Strategy, bluffing, and anxiety all rolled into one.  It's no wonder this game is rated so high. [Gift]  Update:  The more I play, the more I like.  Give it a 10!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[url=http://ballearicus.blogspot.com/2012/02/battle-line.html]Battle Line[/url] es un juego abstracto donde el tema que nos proponen es intrascendente. En este juego las matematicas estan al servicio lúdico. El objetivo que formar combinaciones de cartas, estimaciones de probabilidades (que te salgan aquellas cartas que se necesitan en base la las cartas que quedan en el mazo), cuantas formaciones se pueden formas que superen a la mía en un peón dado, lo hacen muy matemático. Es más, aquel que sea capaz de evaluar mejor estadisticamente todos estos aspectos, será el ganador.

Battle Line tiene cierto aroma a Exploradores. Los jugadores deben comprometerse en la construcción de una formación (o de una expedición) antes de tener certeza de si se podrán completarla. En ambos casos se debe jugar una carta en tu turno, incluso cuando no quieres hacerlo... aunque en Exploradores tienes opción de descartar y esto disminuye la tensión de juego, pese a dar al contrario la opción de robarla. Personalmente prefiero Battle Line a mucha distancia.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
From game to game my opinion of this game bounces between randomfest and awesomesauce. Not that I have anything against randomfests, but I like when the game is up front about it. I probably didn't play it enough or with the right people. 

Tactics cards are cool, but they seem to be far from essential.
The best thing about this game is the tension it creates. Every turn you HAVE to play a card. This will almost always limit your future options, give info to your opponent about what you can and cannot do and more often than not take you closer to defeat. Best part: your opponent plays next with these same problems.

RATING: 10
Outstanding game.  Once you learn the few straight-forward rules, the game is a breeze to play. And yet, it is filled with agonizing decisions almost every play.  It's only detraction is the theme.  We completely ingore it.  Using the formations names instead of just straigt, flush, etc. might actually make the game more confusing.

We don't play with the tactics cards- they're random power takes away any certainty in row control which is key to the game, IMO.

We do play the advanced rule of claiming flags only at the beginning of the turn.  This gives a little more allowance to "throwing off" crap cards in a row you've just lost, and allows for closer, tighter games.

RATING: 7
Pound for pound, this is one of the better two player games. Learning the game takes less than five minutes and game play takes no more than 30 minutes maximum. It is based on a poker set collection mechanic, but, as with Mystery Rummy, the most interesting decisions do not rest on that mechanic (I feel an important point against reductive criticism). The wife loves it because it's close enough to "playing cards" that she can use some of those skills and I love it because I sneak a game which adds surprising depth for such a small package. You really need to contemplate where you want to make a breakthrough and how much you want to tip your hand. I reserve my "10" reviews for "event games" which almost have to be long playing and meaty. The only reason this doesn't rank an "8" or "9" is that the theme bleeds off pretty quickly; we never bothered learning the names of the hands and stuck to the poker terminology, for example. The game doesn't, then, offer any kind of story as does Mystery Rummy or Call of Cthulhu LCG, for example. To its credit, though, it's a tighter, leaner game than either of those with much easier rules. Recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The tactics cards help keep things interesting, but the game is essentially a trick-taking card game where 2 players fight over 9 tricks simultaneously. I like the ease of play, and the short play-time and quick setup make it a quality addition to my lightweight 2-player collection. 

I have not tried the advanced rules, though giving the opponent a chance to respond to your formation before claiming the flag seems like it should be the standard rule in my group. I taught it to a first timer and his immediate question to a claim was "I don't get a chance to respond?" I don't know how shifting the claiming phase to the beginning of the turn makes the game more "advanced". It seems pretty intuitive.

RATING: 8
3 plays:

At first, I felt bogged down with too many possibilities and too much information. Because you have so much information, it can be fruitful, though unpleasant, to take an immense time comparing all of the possibilities. And though the rules themselves are pretty simple, it is complicated to apply the question of whether or not a given battle is winnable; that may well change after a card is played somewhere else, so really you have to be reevaluating many battles after each card is played. Thus the tension and intriguing possibilities don't mount, but merely peter off.

That said, the tactics and player interaction here are wonderfully rich. As long as players are both decently matched (and up for a game that requires lots of strategic thinking), the resulting game is tight and full of subtle tradeoffs. 

So it's not quite the deliciously elegant balance that is Lost Cities, but it's excellent, and I find myself daydreaming about it! 

One more note: the card designs are flat and uninteresting. A colorful re-theme--I'm imagining borrowing the Smallworld brand of fantasy imagery--would add a huge amount.

RATING: 7.4
Easy game to play.  It is similar in some aspects to Rummy and poker.  This is a great warm up game, cool off game or a game to play with someone unfamiliar with gaming.  

Cards are laid in alternating turns in front of line men or game pieces.  Cards laid develop value based on 3 of a kind, straights and flushes.  Line men are won based on the higher values.  The game on top of this value incorporates tactic cards which can disrupt easily your opponets values.

RATING: 10
Phenomenal two player, head to head game that pretty much anyone can learn and have fun with on their first play because of the simplicity of the poker hands. Everyone I've taught this to has loved it.

This personally hits on a lot of the right cylinders for me. There's tension in deciding which cards to put down where. There's tough decisions when you have cards that could play in multiple ways but you have to decide how much of a risk you're willing to take. There's a little bit of bluffing when you only put one card down in front of a flag. And I LOVE the deduction element of proving that someone can't beat your formation and claiming a flag before they can put down 3 cards

UPDATE: I have now played without the Tactics cards and think I prefer Battle Line that way. I'm sure I'll play with them again, but my go-to way is going to be without Tactics cards from now on

RATING: 7.5
When first reading about Battle Line I was interested but put off purchasing it due to what I perceived as being "too simple"; I thought the game might start to feel "old" fast, in other words. However, after deciding to make the purchase and playing my first games, I was very surprised by the amount of depth and strategy involved. I would highly recommend it to anybody who is interested in it, but isn't quite "convinced" enough to buy it yet. It is true that the rules are quite simple, but there are so many different "paths" one can take that I'd say Battle Line is worthy of any and all boardgamers with any sort of interest in strategy games. But due to my experiences with this game, I'd add on top of that that I do truly think that !anybody! can find this game interesting and rewarding. For example, over this past Thanksgiving holiday, I got in nine plays with family members: my mother, grandfather, and uncle. Not only did they each want to immediately play a second game, but all three asked me if I had the game with me the following day to play again! I see this as a very good indicator that Battle Line is a universally enjoyable game.

I generally sleeve my card games, but I haven't felt the need to do this with Battle Line as the cards are very thick and feel of great quality.

Unfortunately, at the time of my writing this, it is out of print and being priced at insanely high numbers (today it is going for $100 on Amazon!); however, I do know that GMT intends to reprint the game within the next few months (and I imagine will continue to do reprint runs far into the future), so it should be easily obtainable for the MSRP of $20 with patience.

I find the game play smooth. The "Tactics" cards seemed a little odd at first, but after playing a few games I can now see how and why the game designer has balanced them the way that they have been.

I rate Battle Line as an 8 because I don't think I will ever turn down an offer to play, but I also feel that if I played it too often on a regular basis I might get "burned out" and may no longer be the one "offering" plays. Don't let that dissuade you from a purchase, though, as I think Battle Line !will! be enjoyed by all and deserves a place in the collection of every boardgamer. I cannot fathom of a reason why I would ever want to get rid of my copy.

:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ A great hand-management filler, where the concept may seem simple (play a card, then draw a card), but it still offer a lot of agonizing choices as around mid game, you don't want to fully display your strategy but you still have to play a card to continue drawing cards to feed your strategy. So you need to choose your battles carefully (the card lanes where you play your cards) in order to win the war (game). This is thus a game with good strategic depth.
++ Relatively small table footprint and really portable game that can be played really almost anywhere.
++ Battle Line feel more thematic than Schotten Totten, because it make more sense to play cards (troops) in a certain ways (formations) to win the battle fields (instead of claiming a stone).
++ This limited edition is really beautiful and well put together and include the Terrain fan expansion, which really add a layer of strategy right from the start when you know the unique battle fields layout.
++ Super easy to teach, super fast to set-up and fast to play.


:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- It's a 2-players only game, and for whatever reason, I don't like buying 2-players only games, even though I play a lot of my game just with my wife.
-- I would have prefer to get a printed box of good quality than just a plain black box with a sticker on it.
-- The number color should have been a bit more saturated and contrasted to easily see the different color in lower light environment. Also, the match between some color and flag shape is not optimal in my opinion, as some color that are alike have flag shape that are also alike. At least one of these two things should always be very different to distinguish card's suits easily at a glance.

:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ For basic comments on Battle Line in general, see my positive points from my "English 7th Edition - Roland's Revenge Games 1st Limited Edition" entry.
++ This edition come with a fully printed box and a fully printed rule book which is really nice.
++ This edition have flag colors that are a bit more over-saturated to easily distinguishing between the 6 colors and the flag shapes are better distributed between the colors (really different flag shape used for similar color).
++ BONUS: We can easily play Arboretum (2-player only) with a Battle Line deck, by only removing the 9-10 card of each suit.


:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- For basic comments on Battle Line in general, See my negative points from my "English 7th Edition - Roland's Revenge Games 1st Limited Edition" entry.
-- The box is really small and even though the cards are now standard sizes (57mm x 89mm), once sleeved with premium Fantasy Flight Standard American Board Game Sleeves, you have to cut 2-3mm of every cards sleeves top to fit them in the box... and the fit is really tight. I would have prefer a bigger box to easily accommodate sleeved cards.

RATING: 8
Players are essentially battling over a row of flags, where victory is decided by whomever can assemble the best fighting formation on their side of each flag.  Some very tough decision making here, as you are fighting over 9 flags simultaneously, so you have all kinds of fronts to think about at the same time!

Tough decisions and a tense game right to the end!  The game can swing in one's favor very unexpectedly, so the battle is never over 'til it's over.

RATING: 8
Updated 5/26/10.

I'm leaving everything that I had previously written up, because it is all true, and I'm keeping Battle Line's rating as a solid 8, because it deserves it.  But since playing Parade two-player, I rarely go back to this game anymore.  Consequently, it is no longer in my Top 10.  That is all.

I'm really surprised about this game.  I prefer board games to card games as a general rule, and I was especially skeptical knowing this card game primarily relies on suited, numbered cards and a modified poker-hand hierarchy.  I bought it mainly thinking that it would a quick, brainless time-killer on days where my wife and I are too tired to even handle Vikings.  

Instead, this turned out to be a really fabulous game.  It's often as tense and AP-inducing as Agricola, but in a 15-minute (actually 45-minute, as we'll play 2-3 games in a row), no-setup package.  

It's not for everyone - it's not terribly deep or strategic.  It's just a really great playing experience.  It's everything I could want in a board game, minus a board, fancy wooden bits, and 2 hours (give or take).  All for $12.

RATING: 10
This really has everything I want in a 2 player game. It's quick and confrontational... it's full of agonizing decisions and the luck factor keeps it extremely replayable. There's a high level of tension here that really makes the hand management game one of the best around (probably my favorite of all time). There's so much to consider on every turn... trying to decide if it's worth it to sacrifice a flag so you can play cards you know your opponent needs in order to claim a flag elsewhere, trying to plan ahead to avoid filling all the spaces up too fast, and trying to figure out how it will hurt you least to play a card when you know it's going to sting no matter what. I'm always, always happy to play this.

Update: This is one of the first two player only games I ever acquired, and still my favorite. I don't think I've ever been like "Nah, let's NOT play BattleLine." So for that 10 = earned.

RATING: 7
My criterion for rating games


http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/147053/item/2300390#item2300390


1) Sense of wonder: This game is the same each time you play it except the cards you draw, but you see most of the cards every game.

2) Meaningful/fun decisions. This game is full of meaningful decisions. Which card to play where is what the game is all about.

3) Player interaction:  For a game of its type it has a lot interaction in playing off of each other's cards and deciding which sets to play on each flag.  

4) Components/artwork:  Great artwork on the cards and nice quality cards.

5) Game length/complexity:  Perfect length game. Not too complex to teach a newbie, but lots of interesting tough decisions and even some use of logic.

RATING: 8
Battle Line is simply one of the best two-player-only card games that I've played. It's straight up better than Lost Cities, and it feels like there's more direct competition, which for me, is a clear positive!

Battle Line shares the same advantage of Lost Cities--on your turn, you play a card, then draw a card. But with Battle Line, you are playing multiple "mini" poker hands against your opponent. This creates a wonderful tension of wanting to get your good cards out, but not sharing too much information too soon.

You can play the game two ways... with or without the tactics cards. They add some interesting surprises and a bit more chaos. 

My wife and I racked up 10+ plays in a matter of two days. This one is definitely a keeper.

RATING: 7
This seems like such a simple design...but Knizia has done it again. This is a wonderful two-player battle of wits. The choice to draw from either deck, and to only be able to play one more Tactics card than your opponent has played provides a great tension and additional dimension of decision-making. 

Some of the information manipulation is reminiscent of Lost Cities, but I believe Battle line has more complexity to it, particularly since you can win with a "break-through" of 3 consecutive flags. There is plenty of strategy to ruminate on during play. That said, it plays breezily and satisfyingly. If you enjoy Knizia games and are looking for 2-player stuff...I'd put Battle Line in the "must have" category.

RATING: 8
Better than Texas Hold 'em.  I'd rather play with Hoplites any day. RATING: 9
Um enorme quebra cabeças para duas pessoas, com roupagem de Poker!
Temos frentes de batalha, e visamos fazer formações (jogos), com 3 cartas cada, mais fortes do que o do oponente para ganhar cada Bandeira individualmente. As formações são as mesmas do poker, straight flush, sequências, etc., com cartas curingas que podem alterar como as formações se comportam. Uma formação só ganha da outra quando fica provado, pelas cartas visíveis, que não tem como o oponente ganhar com a formação imediatamente à frente. Quando consegue 3 bandeiras consecutivas ou 5 alternadas, o jogador vence.
O que deixa mais desafiador é que, necessariamente, temos que jogar uma carta e comprar uma carta após o jogo, muitas vezes precisando sacrificar formações e fazer decisões bem difíceis. Além do fato de que, cada formação, normalmente, só tem 3 slots e não é permitido trocar.

RATING: 7
R's
Another two player game.  Players take turns playing a card then drawing a card.  The goal is to win either 3 contiguous rows or 5 of 9 rows by having the winning set of a max of 3 cards in 9 different rows.  Sets are valued along the lines of poker hands.  Tactics cards can add a bit of viciousness but you can only play one more than the other player has played which mitigates the advantage they can lend.

RATING: 8
Excellent!  Another game I waited too long to try.  2-player battle for flags, using 3-card poker hands.  Straight Flush, 3 of a Kind, Flush, Straight, Chance are the rank of hands, in order of highest to lowest.  Capture 3 adjacent or 5 flags total for the win.

2 plays so far, won both.  First win with 5 and secod with 3 consecutive flags.

RATING: 8.5
Avoided this one for a long time, looked pretty dry and too abstract for me.

Finally bought it as I'm always after 2 player games as I mainly game with my wife.

What a cracking game!

Such a simple game that offers some great tactical decision making right through to the end.  The pleasure gained from claiming a flag based on open information is a mini game of it's own and it is easy to miss a claimed flag if you're not paying attention.

Helen is not too keen on the tactics cards so I only get to play one or two in any given battle but I think she's starting to see the game changing fun of them.

It's hard to argue against this being an abstract game with a pasted on theme.  I do feel however that although there is no narrative theme the feeling of a battle for the line of flags is strong and you feel like you're making ground with each small victory.

Highly recommended to both new and seasoned gamers, a must have for any collection.

Plays include pseudo battle line:
Use a 6 card hand, standard deck of playing cards consisting of 4 suits using 1(A)-J(11). Remove two cards at random (I played hidden but could be done open if desired) at the outset. Play for 7 points, end when neither opponent can play.

Ranking of hands: St.Fl., 3 of a kind, St., Fl, R.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This 2-player strategy game has some definite interesting points.  You want to win one of 9 flags by eventually laying down the best 3-card combination (straight flush is best, followed by 3 of a kind, and so on).  But you can't claim the flag until you can prove - based on the cards already out there - that you can't be beat.

In a way this is playing Balloon Cup where you try to "lock" a tile, but Battle Line adds the "must prove" element so it's not so simple.

I think I liked it, but would need to play it more to get underneath the strategies.  I did find that the discussions/arguments about proving you can win a flag get a little too nasty for my taste.  Maybe not the best game to play against the one I love!

RATING: 8
Looking forward to playing this again.

The mechanics 'play one, draw one' are identical to Lost Cities, though the toys are very different as are the rules.

There's a similar sense of 'constantly showing your hand', making playing 2nd probably advantageous. 

The multiple victory conditions keep it exciting - giving a constant chance of victory and stopping the game if it ever becomes too hopeless. Playing against an experienced player, I never felt as hopeless as I did  during my 2nd game of Lost Cities against Gregor. 

There's obviously a lot of strategy to learn in placing numbers correctly and a bit of luck in the tactics cards. A better player can choose to nearly-eliminate the luck factor if they want though, thanks to the rule that you can only play one more than your opponent - a brilliant masterstroke.

Frankly, I think my mind is blown by how good this is.

RATING: 9
This is a great game. I heard it described as comparable to Lost Cities, and now that I've played it I don't really agree. The two have some superficial similarities, but I feel Battle Line is better in every way. It's fast, there are lots of tough decisions to make and mind-games to play with your opponent. The tactics cards add some great high-powered weapons to your arsenal, but the rules cleverly allow your opponent to restrict your use of these cards, if they wish. I love the theme, too, despite being almost totally disconnected to the game. This game is cheap and widely available - try it!

RATING: 9
I love the pacing of this game. Every decision gets more and more stressful and tense, and the whole game builds until it reaches a climactic end. No points counting, no guessing who's in the lead. When it ends, it just ends.

It also has a surprisingly fitting theme. When I first played, it I thought it was really pasted on, but after a bit of reflection, it really does perfectly convey the tension of a battle (not a war). The feeling of having your perfectly formed line slowly collapse, and knowing you have a limited supply of reinforcements, and having to make hard compromises about your formation given the time pressure. No other war-themed game I've played has captured that tension and stress nearly as well.

RATING: 8.3
[November 2015 - 8.3]
Fine-tuning the rank.

[April 2015 - 8.4]
Moves up a couple of notches. It's a great little mechanic that should be part of more games.  You'll love and hate it at the same time.

[October 2014 - 8]
Such a simple game, yet it can illicit a lot of thought and emotions (highs and lows) throughout the game. And while there's little theme, there is that sense of trying to break your forces through the line. My suggestion when you play, don't over-think it. I really enjoy this game while waiting at an airport or in a bar -- you can put as much thought into it as you feel like. One house rule we play with is to allow the discard of a card into a battle that has already been resolved but still has an open space for a card. That way the final battles are still competitive without devolving into what amount to forfeits because you have to place a useless card in an ongoing battle.

RATING: 9
Idea:
Play cards in different rows to form combinations of numbers/colors to beat opponents combination in same row. Win 5/9 rows or 3 adjacent rows to win game

Mechanism:
Both players play card at a time (1-10, 6 colors) out of 7 in hand to form strongest(=hardest) combinations. Real key to the game are 10 special cards that can be drawn instead of normal card BUT you can play only 1 more special card than your opponent. Thus if you play one, you cant play more unless your opponent does! That makes really much more possibilities for strategies.

to who:
Awesome game, better than Lost Cities by far! Really strong 1on1 game. One of my wifes favourites and is very easy to take on when travelling.

RATING: 10
Easily one of my favorite two-player games. Easy to teach (recommend creating simple reference sheet for the different formations,) but deceptively deep strategy (Lost Cities to the extreme.) As you must play a card every turn, you're constantly forced to reveal information to your opponent. You don't want them to know that you've got a powerful hand planned for the middle row, but are going to throw some garbage on the rightmost one - but you need to put down something.

I couldn't recommend this game enough to anyone whose got regular opportunity for 2 player games - Knizia knows how to take a large serving of strategy, add a dash of luck, and produce amazing results.

RATING: 5
This is an interesting game to move up from always playing Lost Cities. Basically, you're trying to build competing piles of cards (almost like small poker hands) in different areas to win pawns. Good interaction and deduction possibilities, and the special cards add a little unpredictability. 

The downside is that it becomes like the scene in The Princess Bride ("so clearly I cannot choose the glass in from of you, except...") where some players may just decide there's too much analysis to be done for a game of this weight.

Also: an extra demerit for the lousy clip-art on the cards. Bad art! No biscuit!

I ended up trading it away.

RATING: 10
Update: This game has truly earned the rating of 10, as I play it so much I do not even consider my fairly extensive two player game collection before suggesting Battle Line. If you buy one two player card game make it Battle Line.

This game of numbers and colors is Knizia at his very best. 

It's still a very good game with impressive depth and replayability. It's not over until the end, have won and lost many a game after getting far ahead or behind. 

Another testament to its staying power is that I can't win. I probably lose about 3 of 4 lately. For whatever reason this intrigues me. I guess I like punishment. 

Update 2: I am no longer a strong proponent of not using the advanced rule. The game is strong either way, I still prefer no advanced rule for the sake of consistency, but I would play with it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Playing this reminds me of the old saw about pro basketball, that you only need to watch the last five minutes because that's when the game will always be decided.  In many games of Battle Line, the game seems to be decided on who is able to draw the cards they need at the very end.  But the end game is a reflection of the foundation.  Smart early play will create small advantages at the end - sure, you can still be beaten by lucky draws now and then, but you'll win more than you lose if you plan it out properly.  Every card played gives information to your opponent and limits your future opportunities, so each turn can be beautifully agonizing.

The game does lack interaction between the players' card draws.  That's why I prefer the tactics cards, despite the lack of balance.  Also, the advanced scoring rules are helpful, because they encourage the use of the tactic cards, which otherwise seem to never get played by whichever player feels like they have a slight advantage.

I'm enjoying this and it seems to go over well with everyone I introduce it to.  The best filler I have come across.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm always on the lookout for short, clever two-player games for my wife and I to play. Battle Line fits the bill.

The poker-style set comparison is easy to learn. The ability to hide your intent until the second card of three is played creates opportunities for misdirection.

The game does not offer much variety. Its strength is in the simplicity of its mechanic. I recommend it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
English version of German Schotten Totten (see flexgames.com).  One of the most popular light two-player games, more fun and more interesting choices than Lost Cities.  It certainly packs considerable tension into a small game, although there is potential for AP.  Very tense and agonizing decisions!  Has elements reminiscent of Lost Cities, Balloon Cup, Poker, and Rummy.  Haven't yet played with the Tactics cards, but I can imagine they would mitigate the luck element, which can be slightly frustrating (sometimes it's down to both players hoping to draw a certain card which will complete a formation).  We currently play with the advanced rule or official variant that flags can only be claimed at the very beginning of your turn before playing a card (which allows opponent a turn to react by dumping a card or using Tactics cards), rather than the original rule that flags can be claimed during your turn after playing a card but before drawing a card.

Find Schotten Totten too constrained, so prefer Battle Line, though usually play without Tactics.

Have several custom decks, including [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/40596/item/2092720#item2092720]Ancients[/url], and [url=www.boardgamegeek.com/listitem/918978?commentid=2822580#comment2822580]Asterix[/url].

RATING: 7

An evil little game, filled with many tough decisions. The design incorporates hand management, with a significant level of decision angst. Simple to learn, difficult to master, always good as a filler between heavier games. Is Battleline a wargame? Well, I'd say "no." More accurately, it's poker with a warlike theme pasted on. Battles over objectives are fough using poker hands. 

If you like the Lost Cities card game, do yourself a favor and try this one out too! If you like quick playing brain burners, try this game immediately!

RATING: 6
Set matching poker style. This game is strategic, all card driven and has a set of cards used for breaking the rules with a catch, you can only play 1 more than your opponent. The game plays as a sort of capture the flag match, once a flag is claimed, it can't be taken away, and cards that break the rules (tactics cards) cannot be used to determine that a flag can't be claimed. All one needs to do is prove they have one the flag without the chance to lose their claim and it's theirs.

RATING: 6
Battle Line is Schotten Totten with the addition of tactic cards.  The strength of Battle Line comes in the quick tactical play; decisions are not always obvious in this game as second guessing, growing information and luck all play a role in designing the next best move.  Similarities are often drawn between this and Lost Cities, and although I understand the claims both games to me play vastly different.  Battle Line doesn't force specific suits to be played on assigned piles so therefore creates the feeling of more control than Lost Cities. Where as in most games this would be a good thing, in a card game of this ilk I prefer the tense limitations of Lost Cities.  That's not to suggest Battle Line is a bad game, quite the contrary.  I enjoy Battle Line as a strong two player outing, but I will always call upon it's sibling if given the choice.

RATING: 8
Have you every thought, hey wouldn't it be fun if there was a game which involved playing 9 hands of 3 card poker at the same time? Battle Line did it... and it's fantastic!

Battle line is a 2 player card game that pits two ancient generals against each other in tactical battle. Players must create combos of cards that out rank their opponent. When you complete a set of 3 you check to see if you can beat your opponent's combo. If your opponent can not outrank your combo, you'll win that flag. Out of the 9 flags you need 3 adjacent or 5 total to win the game.

Strategy and luck meld almost perfectly. You'll always be thinking about your next move, but be hoping desperately to get that one card you need. The game is indirectly competitive (aside from tactics cards). It's more about setting up your own combos rather than attacking your opponent directly which I like.

It's strategic and simple to pick up. It's competitive, but not too fierce. Battle Line balances all the aspects I love in board games. It will always have a special place on my shelf.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
More choices than Lost Cities, one round is longer than a round of Lost Cities though.

RATING: 8
I usually find my leg unconsciously jittering while playing this game due to the immense tension.  Will I draw the card I need?  Which meld do I use this card for?  Do I commit a flag by playing a 2nd card or do I stall in hopes of something better by playing to another flag?  My only complaint is that, since you have to play/commit cards immediately, you almost always have to hope it's you who draws the card which completes the meld.  I usually play without the special cards as the basic game is excellent as it is.

RATING: 7
[b][i]Battle Line[/i][/b] is a masterpiece of simplicity in design. It is somewhat similar to Lost Cities in scope and feel, but I prefer BL thanks to my perception that it is more strategical and less luck based than Lost Cities. BL is, however, "just" a simple card game and thus doesn't have the variety of strategies that characterize better-ranked board games.

The Tactics cards make Battle Line much better than its predecessor Schotten-Totten.

RATING: 4
A simple set collection game - draw cards and attempt to build poker style sets (three of a kind, straight, flush) in one of five available colours in front of nine available "battle" sectors. Luck/press your luck is a major factor as is card counting and assessing the odds of getting that last card you need as the game progresses. Cards are good quality, although the art is only Ok.

RATING: 8
[b] Battleline[/b] [Reiner Knizia 2000[/i] :star: Print and Play Version
Good travel game and fantastic fast two players filler,  Knizia makes a simple idea works flawlessly, It is one of the most clever card games I had ever played.  An amazing battle with easy a simple to grasp rules in which players compete for the best play conquering banners. The first one to take five of them (or three in a row) is the winner of the game. I guess this is the simplest game in the top 100 but is still a sure winner. Easy to take away, is also a perfect choice for travels and trips, as long as you plan to play with only two persons. The cards have a good quality and the game is not very pricey (it can even be easily P&Ped but I dont think this is a need since a retails copy costs less than 15 euros). I own a retail copy and one redesign.

RATING: 9
Battle Line is one of the great two player card games. Instead of rummy inspirations, Battle Line has you playing poker hands of three cards along nine different locations. It is rewarding to take a location by proving a hand of yours cannot be beat. Additionally, the tactic cards through in the possibility of countering opponent's plans. Everything is very well balanced, and it is a rare game where players are playing against each other instead of competing to be the most efficient at an activity.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

RATING: 8
rating after one play - often seen described as Lost Cities for gamers.  This has a very different feel to it to me.  you play a card then draw a card just like in Lost Cities but the similarities end there.  You've got more categories in which you are competing. You've also got what are called formations, which are sets of cards kind of like rummy melds.  These formations are ranked so that some are more powerful than others.  So, while in Lost Cities, whoever has the most points in an expedition (i.e. a suit), in Battle Line you have more options with the formations.  Furthermore, in Lost Cities, your cards in expedition A are only competing against expedition A on your opponents side of the table.  Here, you play your cards wherever you find them to be most useful to you.  For example, one formation is called a batallion which is a set of cards of the same color (suit).  You may be competing for in a category for a flag against another batallion of the same color, or even a different color, or against another formation entirely such as a phalanx which is a higher rank formation.  And finally, you have tactics cards that give you special abilities.  In conclusion (to make this sound completely like a compare/contrast essay for a high school english class)...  Totally different games.  I like both.  Battleline has many more options so there is more room for tactical/strategic gameplay.

Edit: This game is f'in brilliant. I believe at this point that this game is Knizia's masterpiece. After I bought it I played fifteenish games in two days. This game being as good as it is without a reliance on a credible theme makes me question my allegience to the Ameritrash. The bluffing of having cards you don't, the paranoia of others having cards that you need, the desperation of needing THAT ONE CARD that will win you the game. Amazing. The game is so accessible, my non-gamer dad read the rules in five minutes and put up a helluva fight. But there's also so much strategy, you will constantly be learning little tricks to improve your game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Battle Line is a re-theme of a basic mathematical game, one which Knizia has returned to more than once with freshly painted theme.

But for many this is the best implementation- and having played at least the Narnia re-theme I think I agree with that sentiment.

Basically, players pile card hands opposite score tokens, trying to win either 3 immediately adjacent tokens or 5 of 9 tokens to claim victory.

So what makes this version special- what makes a game so potentially simple special?

First, the theme works here- the feeling of warriors rushing the line of battle, and perhaps turning the tide of battle actually invokes a little feeling of the Iliad, and the art is nice, but not fanciful, so it retains more of a sense of realism than a tug of war between Scots or Caspian's fantasy battle with his Regent do.

The tactics/rule change cards also seem more realistic in terms of effect and play, so that players can change the fate of tokens in more strategic manner.  This makes the game play a little more interesting.

The formations/hand rankings also make a little more sense with the Battle Line artwork/theme.

If a themeless Knizia eurogame is not your thing, this is one of the better themed games that he has created.

Plus it plays quickly and easily, and it invites a rematch when the battle is done.

Battle Line represents a nice balance of theme and game for me, and it doesn't overstay it's welcome.

This is the kind of game that Knizia built his reputation on- a solid, replayable game, which rewards players and which wants to come out again and again.

RATING: 9.88
I was too long in appreciating Battle Line. I scoffed when Calandale said it was a good wargame, I played it and lost to my wife but didn’t consider it anything special.

Then I won. And it wasn’t that I had won, it was that I realized why I had won. And I understood that the game wasn’t only about getting a breakthrough or an envelopment; it was about focusing your strength on your strongest points, about using that push to outwait your enemy until he commited forces to an unopposed front: and then you one-upped him there. You concentrated your forces, left as many options free as you could, tried to get him to commit before you did. You made sure that you didn’t fail to send forces in where you could before the enemy took the high point, and you made sure you could win at the end of the battle by holding back reserves until you knew what the enemy had thrown against you on your unopposed fronts. And then you chose your reserves and slaughtered him.

And suddenly it was about breakthroughs and envelopments again. Holding back reserves for the final stroke meant launching a strong push now; waiting out the enemy to send in the reinforcements against his committed troops was the way to an envelopement.

I can’t get enough of Battle Line. I used to snigger at the acolytes of Reiner Knizia; I am still no Knizian, but I see where they’re coming from. This game alone earned him a place in my hall of great designers. That it is published by GMT is only the cream on top of the chowder.[color=#ffffff]a9Z4MnAx[/color]

RATING: 7
I've disliked many of the other two-player card games which are often mentioned in the same breath with this one, so I was surprised to find that Battle Line was quite enjoyable. It's a clever tug-of-war with several winning conditions, in which you must balance between preventing your opponent from winning and laying your own plans. What makes it interesting is that you have a pretty high degree of control, and choose when you want to draw cards and which type of card to draw.  Liked it, would play again. (1 play)

RATING: 7
Cada jugador debe situar 9 formaciones de 3 cartas a lo largo de una línea de batalla (Battle Line). Cada formación está opuesta a la del contrario y el resultado depende de la fuerza de esta formación, que viene ordenada en una jerarquía similar a la del poker (escalera de color> poker> color> escalera> tres cartas cualesquiera). Ganará el jugador que tenga 3 formaciones ganadoras seguidas o 5 no contiguas. Todo esto jugado con un mazo de 60 cartas de 6 colores numeradas del 1-10 + 10 cartas de tácticas que alteran las formaciones de alguna manera especial. PROS Elementos en común con el Poker o Chinchón que le hacen fácil de aprender. El método deductivo para demostrar que serás el ganador de una jugada con independencia de lo que pudiera llegar a jugar el contrario. CONS Seco y abstracto con factor suerte por encima de la media. Cualquier cuentacartas lo encontraría sofisticado y analítico, pero yo no soy así.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
The popular view is that Battle Line is a better version of Lost Cities. I have to agree with that. I still like Lost Cities, but I really don't want to play it if I can play Battle Line. Improvements include: the game is very slightly faster, has direct player opposition instead of being two player solitaire, and it doesn't have the weird scoring system that confuses people when they play it the first time.
The one downside is that it doesn't look nearly as nice as Lost Cities. 

Still love Battle Line, but it's been pretty well replaced by East-West from Knizia's book Blazing Aces.

Began playing again, using the tactics cards a bit more, and moved it up to 10. The tactics cards change it enough that nothing else quite matches it.

RATING: 9
Knizia's version of Chinese Poker. Excellent design, where everything feels very tight with nothing that could be tweaked. Full of agonizing choices and planning ahead to counter potential threats, like my favorite abstracts. Reminds me of the traditional games of Piquet and Mate in that way too--but better.  Card counting and probability integral, of course.  I thought the tactic cards would ruin the game; but they blend in due to the clever limitations Knizia has imposed (e.g. you can't play two more than opponent has played, which adds another tactical move to the game). It's great that you can play the game without them depending on your mood.  I love how you can forestall a tactic card by claiming a flag early. Don't insult this game by calling it a "filler."
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Brilliant little 2 player trick taking game with a bit of theme on top that actually works.

Its basically making the best poker hand you can with the hand of cards you have by placing one card down at a time in turn, once you have three cards down that section is locked down and who ever has the best 3 card formation wins

For example you can have three cards of the same colour (Flush in poker) vs three of increasing value (Straight in poker)

Then you also get Tactics cards which can bend certain rules of the game.

RATING: 9.5
[>5 plays] Nine individual battles fought by placing mini poker hands by each flag. Win three-in-a-row or five total to win the game. It's important to leave yourself as flexible as possible for as long as possible because you won't get most of the cards you want. The tactics cards make for a variation to play; some are more powerful than others, so taking one may result in a useless card sitting in your hand. It's another one of my girlfriend's favorites.

RATING: 6
Un juego divertido para dos personas. Las partidas son cortas y con ganas de revancha. Es una lucha por conseguir 5 banderas (ó 3 adyacentes) mediante la combinación más alta de 3 cartas a cada lado de cada bandera, siendo estas combinaciones tipo poker.

Además, puedes usar cartas tácticas que permiten romper formaciones ó usarlas como sustituto de cartas para completar una formación.

No es un juego nada complicado, pero las partidas tienen cierto punto de tensión.
[color=blue]
Funny game for two people. Games are short and with a little revenge flavour. You battle for getting 5 flags (or 3 adjacent flags) trying to combine 3 cards, winnig the highest one at both sides of each flag. These combinations are poker based.

Besides, you can use tactic cards that will allow you to break combinations or to use them for completing one.

It is an easy cardgame, but games are very competitive.
[/color]

RATING: 8
A classic Knizia game of playing 3-card poker hands to either claim 5 of 9 flags or 3 consecutive flags. Tension of tension in this game.  When do I go hard for a flag versus when do I cut my losses.  When do I draft a tactics card?  When do I play my tactics card? =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Comparisons to Lost Cities made me steer clear of Battle Line for a long while. It's a much, much better game than Lost Cities, and yet, I understand the comparison. In both games, you have to pick your battles as you play cards to several points along a line. In each game, you try to make informed choices about how ambitious you can be with a particular batch of cards, how long you can delay before committing yourself further, etc. Battle Line just does all this with four times the depth of Lost Cities, and yes, a more apt theme. Sure, the gameplay is fairly abstract, but I can see outmaneuvering an opponent with superior formations a lot faster than I can see archeological expeditions. There are a lot of terrible couples games out there. This one is the real McCoy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Lost Cities' more sophisticated cousin -- has that similar feel of having to commit to a plan without knowing whether it'll pan out.  Much more variety, though, than Lost Cities thanks to the tactics cards.

I really like the ebb and flow of this game.  You start out with high hopes, aiming for strong positions, and then start scrambling, trying to make the most of an always imperfect situation.  My Dad hated it -- I think he didn't feel he had enough control.

I love Knizia's ability to subtly evoke a real-life situation with fairly simple rules and abstract pieces.  I get great satisfaction when three lowly infantry are able to defeat three mighty elephants because the infantry have a stronger formation.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Number of players: 2

Components: (7)
I don't find the artwork to be particularly attractive, but the cards are good quality and easy to read and distinguish from one another.  
  
Rules: (10)
The rules are simple, clearly and logically written. 

Setup: (10)
You just have to play a number of “flag” pawns and make sure that they’re at least a card width away from each other.

Area required to play: (8)
This game doesn’t require a massive area to play, but more than you may expect. You’ll have nine columns, each will eventually hold approximately six cards (three to either side).
 
Fun: (7)
I enjoy this game. I understand why it’s been around as long as it has been, and expect that it’ll be around for much long.

Theme Integration / Mechanics: (7)
It works. This game could have just as easily be abstract. It could have also been about anything at all. It could have had a science fiction or fantasy theme! That would have been great.

Replay Value: (9)
Even though this isn’t my favorite game in the world, it’s one that I’m glad to have and one that I’m sure will be played many times throughout the years.

Storage: (8)
The box that this game comes in is about twice the size that it needs to be to house everything comfortably, but it’s still a small box and the insert does a nice job of keeping everything sorted and read for the next play.

Accessibility: (9)
The rules could be slightly confusing to some, but not daunting by any measure. If a person can play poker, a person can play this game. 

Over-All/Bonuses/Biases:  7.5

RATING: 7.5
A very nice strategy card game.  There is some luck to the draw of the cards but ultimately the strategy shines through with the decision how and when to play your cards which will ultimately determine how successful you are in winning flags.  The tactics cards are very few but they make the game infinitely more strategic and tense and really add to the joy of the game.  I agree that there is little theme to the game, the play would feel very similar if the deck had diamonds/spades/hearts/clubs on them but this does not hinder the enjoyment of a quick easy to learn but complex to play game.  

Update: dropped rating from 9 to 7.5 after 14th play.  Starting to think this game really just comes down to the luck of the draw.

RATING: 8
While I'm not a huge fan of the 'luck of the draw,' this game is still a ton of fun and now ranks among my favorite 2 player games. Since you're building sets (of colors, straights, or both) and you need to allocate these sets to 9 possible battle positions it can be tough deciding how to 'deploy' your forces because the wrong deployment at the wrong time can tip your opponent off which way you're going for the win and that gives them an edge to counter (providing they have the cards needed). For the longest time I didn't see what all the talk was about, even after I bought it and read the rulesI didn't see it. It wasn't until I actually played it the first time that I realized *why* this ranks high for a lot of gamers, including myself now. I hope you may enjoy it as much as I do!

RATING: 6
Schotten-Totten with ten-card suits, seven-card hands, and special cards that shake up the strategic probability management of the original gameplay with tactical surprises. I haven't played this enough to say whether the changes aside from the tactics cards are significantly better or worse, but I suspect they are unnecessary. I do know I don't care for the tactics cards which shove the balance between knowledge and uncertainty to a wacky place it really had no business going. You can of course use Schotten-Totten rules with these components and it would be my recommendation to do so most of the time. Also make sure you always play with the "advanced rule" of claiming at the beginning of your turn.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
CRITIQUE OF BATTLE LINE
I'm not a big fan of Knizia's games, because they are often sold with the promise of an interesting theme (setting off on an expedition in Lost Cities, fighting for control of Japan in Samurai) and yet the game itself is just a math exercise. The same could be said for Battle Line, because the detailed and interestingly named cards in the game have nothing to do with the gameplay.

Flexibility is the key to success in BL, but how you go about this is up to you. Playing one card next to a flag starts the formation, but keeps it flexible. Playing the second card locks you in to a particular formation. This makes you vulnerable to one of those "you can't win this one" losses. So keeping some formation open as long as possible can be a good strategy, but this can be offset by the fact that it forces you to lock in formations on other flags. And then there are the flags where you dump your bad cards, and you want to keep those open as long as possible.

The dynamics brought by the tactics cards are another interesting addition, and they dump even more choices onto your plate. Often I will never draw one, because this limits my opponent to a single draw from the pile. It's risky, but a draw from the regular deck can be just as valuable as a draw from the tactics deck. On the other hand, I've also played games in which the tactics pile was emptied fairly early on and every card came into play. Again, how you do this is up to you, and these cards add a nice twist to the game.

The thing that you have to remember is that even though the formations look like poker hands, in Battle Line the entire deck can be drawn into the players' hands. This means that you actually have a 50% chance of seeing any card. So it isn't that unlikely to hope that you will draw the card that completes a critical formation (even if it's the last ten in the deck). I love this aspect of the game, because there is tension with every card drawn. Fantastic.

As you can no doubt tell, I think this game gets most everything right. So why not rate it a 10? It's not a 10 because the tension actually decreases toward the end of the game, and the important thing about the game, the choices you make, ceases to be a factor at all. Every choice is important at the beginning, but as time goes on the luck of the draw starts to take over, and in the end luck will often decide the victor. Make no mistake; Battle Line is a good game, but this is what keeps it from being great.

RATING: 8
Full of agonizing choices- too often, no good play seems available, and you have to dump a card and hope it didn't just clinch the game for your opponent, but the person across the table has the same problem. RATING: 7
Strategical capture the flag with a poker hierarchy. Players take turns playing cards at various strategical places vying for a contested flag. The player with the best formation (read as poker hand) wins the flag. First to 5, or first to 3 adjacent flags wins. A lot of good strategy involved in this game. Some variance with card drawing, but otherwise plenty to think about with this one. Very fun 2 player game that can be played very quickly. Definitely willing to give this one many more plays.

RATING: 10
My BGG.CON 2008 prize table game!  Not sure how I feel about it yet, but I was surprised at how surprised I was at how abstract it is.

Update: after a few more plays, this has really grown on me.

Update: this is now probably one of my favorite 2-player non-wargames.  I guess I could burn out on this after a while, but I can't believe how much I didn't like it the first time I played it.

2009-10-31: Moving this up to a 10.  The player who goes first seems to lose significantly more often, but that doesn't bother me much: it just makes a win that much more brutal when you went first, and a loss that much more painful when you went second.

Update: while cleaning up the game, I managed to pour a gallon of coffee into the box, ruining everything, including a really neat set of custom Tactics cards the burrito boy had made for me as a birthday present.  In my embarrassment I just picked up the whole thing & dumped it in the trash; later I wished I'd tried to save the cards the boy made for me, as they couldn't be replaced.  (I did later get a replacement copy--thanks leroy43!)

[b]"Basic" vs. "Advanced":[/b] I prefer the "basic" rule (claim flags at the end of your turn) over the optional "advanced" rule (claim flags at the start of your turn) because the game is meaner that way.  When you're in a position to claim a flag, "advanced" gives your opponent a chance to save their bacon if they happen to be holding an applicable Tactics card, or discard a card into the hopeless flag, making the game softer & more forgiving.  Pshhh!  (Poll [thread=410176]here[/thread])

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Two opponents face off across a 'battle line' and attempt to win the battle by taking 5 of 9 flags or 3 adjacent flags. Flags are decided by placing cards into 3 card poker-type hands on either side of the flag (similar to straight flush, 3 of a kind, straight, flush, etc). The side with the highest 'formation' of cards wins the flag.

RATING: N/A
Totally fascinating on so many levels. But I can see how the game might be even better with the original rules from Schotten-Totten (no '10' cards, hand limit of 6, no Tactics cards, flags can only be claimed at the beginning of your turn). The changes made in Battle Line were implemented as a marketing strategy intended to make the game more appealing to an American audience. Not that I'm complaining: the Tactics cards are really fun and well-designed. But the "pure" game without them is most likely tighter strategy-wise, and perhaps more subtle.

RATING: 8
Card game was not regarded as a serious enough game by gamers back then.  Things now change as the popularity of the use of cards grew.  Also, it appears that it has a sound and working system...unlike other card play problems in other card games, e.g. one of the very own is designer's Ivanhoe.  This game is actually a poker in disguise, but with slight twists of course.  The normal suit orders follow (they are given the formation names in the game for the theme though) and if they are tied in suits, the higher total no. claims the flags.  You win by claiming any 5 flags out of a total 9 or 3 ones if they are adjacent to each other.  Some tactics cards change opponent's deployment so you might see one flank suddenly collapses. A good time-filler in a club day.

Note: The game is so good that I bought the iPhone app.  Since then, numerous and countless gameplays.  I have lost count of the number of the games played as I play it often to fill the time during travel, in the washroom, or at nights before sleep.....yes, I don't feel getting any tired of it. The count of the gameplay here in BGG refers to the original version by GMT and face to face plays.

RATING: 9
Perhaps my favorite 2P game - the fact that it has so much in common with a mainstream game (Poker) makes the learning curve substantially easier. The tactics cards certainly shake things up somewhat, but there's still nothing like seeing the opponent realize that the game is not only about making poker hands, but about MAINTAINING open options to place cards - that is the key to the game. Whomever has to start 'destroying' hands first, losing options, typically loses the game.

RATING: 9
Una estrategia inicial puede consistir en no enseñar demasiado pronto al rival qué combinación es la que vas a poner tras una bandera; así, el rival no sabrá a qué hacer frente y de esta manera tú podrás reaccionar de una u otra manera según la carta que el rival jugó.

Otra opción consiste en no poner cartas delante de una posible muy buena combinación del rival de manera que éste tenga la victoria segura en esa bandera. Es mejor colocar cartas en banderas vacías tanto por el rival como por ti.

Tampoco es bueno tener en tu mano demasiadas cartas tácticas, ya que es posible que no puedas utilizarlas todavía y además te impiden tener más cartas de tropa que quizá te serían de utilidad en la partida. Esta opción sólo es viable si pretendes evitar que el jugador rival tenga tal o cual carta de táctica.
Por lo general, 1 ó 2 cartas de táctica en tu mano de juego es lo más óptimo. 

Saber qué cartas de táctica están por salir puede ser muy útil para afrontar una estrategia u otra.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A beautiful example of how you can build a classic game with a very simple rule set.  What you’re doing in Battle Line is essentially no more than playing 9 simultaneous hands of poker; the hand management mechanic is no more than building the best combination of three cards.  But the way you have to build them based on your opponent’s actual and predicted moves, combined with a solid theme and an element of card counting, makes it suspenseful as hell.

The theme is well-executed, with simple but handsome art, and while it’s not especially deep, it does remind you of the strategic elements of warfare.  The Tactics cards throw in a bit of wildness, as well, to keep things interesting.  Takes up a bit of space, but otherwise a perfect portable 2-player game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I don't see this replacing [GAMEID=50] completely, and especially not as an "airport game".  [GAMEID=760] feels different from other light card games such as [GAMEID=50], [GAMEID=6137], and two player [GAMEID=11].  Those games are more light and more about risk management, while [GAMEID=760] feels a lot more tactical and analytical.  It can be easy to fall into analysis paralysis here.  It's much more about weighing the probabilities and trying to better the position your cards against your opponent.  As a result, it can sometimes feel reactive as the best move is often to simply wait and see what your opponent plays.  It also misses the control of draw deck tempo and the delicious tension of possibly discarding and giving your opponent valuable cards in [GAMEID=50].  Surprisingly, I would say that [GAMEID=50] can often feel like you're more in control because you do have that control over temp and how much of the deck you actually see.  In [GAMEID=760], what cards you see are pre-determined from the start and your goal is just to make the best of it.  I'm not trying to bag on [GAMEID=760]---we actually really do enjoy it, and it really does have a feel of directly facing off against your opponent in a battle line.  Although just a card game, it adds in a spatial component over where you should play your cards.  Two different, good games that have a place in our library.  One is to take with us as a low-stress game in airports, the other is for an evening of brain burning.

As for the Tactics card debate...surprisingly, we're strongly on the side of the original Schotten Totten rules with no Tactics cards and 1-9 card values.  It seems like usually we prefer games having a lot of different actions to choose from, but there's something very clean and strategic about the original rules that works for us.

RATING: 5
This game definitely has a very high level of tactical play, even if one does not consider it does so in a very short time, and with rules that can be basically taught to anyone. However, when I thin of the fun value, it doesn't hold up as strongly. It is extremely abstract; there is very little hiding that you are basically just playing cards of different suits and numbers (albeit with a couple of special cards). To me at least it lacks tension because there is no real tug of war for each individual part of the "battle line", instead, as you play cards you reveal what you can't do, and your opponent either has a chance to beat it or they don't. Therefore, it never feels like "oh god, I need to concentrate here to save this part of the battle line", even if you actually do tactically by saving cards to ensure better sets in that area. Finally, despite its simplicity there is actually a decent amount of overhead because to play optimally, you need to see what are your possible combinations based on every single card on the table.

RATING: 10
This game shares alot of qualities with Lost Cities and if you like one you'll probably like the other, but for my money Battle Line is the better game.  Lost Cities is tighter and quicker with better game production, but Battle Line seems like it has a little more meat on its bones.  The nine flags give you a little more room for recovery and the tactic cards are surprisingly unbroken and add an interesting twist.  Like Lost Cities I really like the ratio of known    to unknown information and how it changes the more you get into the game.       Towards the end of the game when it becomes more and more obvious what you can or can't do as cards are laid down tension really begins to build and the game mechanics shine.  This isn't my favorite game to play, but until I play a better two player game this gets a 10.  I feel like Lord of the Ring: Confrontation might knock it down, but until then...

Very interesting game that balances skill and luck at the same time. Of course extreme amounts of luck can win you a game (five 8-9-10 "royal flushes"), but most of the time, the key to victory is to squash your opponent's formation with a slightly better one on your side. Careful use of tactics cards will reverse the tide of the game, which is crucial when you're getting "cannon fodder" cards from the draw pile.

Best played with an experienced player, as the game will be highly vicious (and fun!).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Simple, enjoyable, yet deep. An excellent 2 player game that plays within 30'.

Each player manages a total of 9 formations along a battle line. Each of your formation fights against the formation on the opposite side of the battle line. The outcome of the battle depends on the type of the opposing formations: the strongest formation type wins. Much like poker :-)

The types of formations are indeed very similar to poker hands (straight flush, 3 of a kind, flush etc). In Battle Line each formation has 3 cards from a deck of 6 colors and you manage 9 hands concurrently.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

特殊カード入れたらこのシンプルさがどう変わるのかやってみたい。

RATING: 10
