=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a simple game, but it has A LOT of depth.

At first, I thought "Ok: set collection and hand management. Let's do this". But, as the game progressed, I was like: "WOW, there's a lot going on here! I have to think this through as there are so many possible tactics to be applied".

On the downside, it's abstract. You don't feel yourself as a general trying to break through a battle line as the name and the cards try to suggest, but as a person trying to make sets of cards and place them in a better way than your enemy.

Overall, this is a very good 2-player game. It's much heavier than it suggests, and it's abstract.

RATING: 5
Battle Line fits in a weird spot for me. While Knizia's Lost Cities is simple enough to be played passively as a filler when engaging your attention in some other activity (converstation, watching television, etc.), Battle Line is slightly more complex and requires a higher level of attention to detail. The downside of this is that I do not find the gameplay in Battle Line interesting enough to warrant focusing all of my energy toward playing it as I would another head-to-head card game or abstract strategy.

RATING: 9
This has everything I want from a Knizia game: short play time, incredible depth:size ratio, tough decisions every turn, evil math, and a surprising amount of strategy for what it is. I love it.

The two win conditions plus the need to weigh holding a card so as not to tip your hand on a flag vs. committing a card somewhere useful vs. keeping a number as a number instead of using it for color vs. so many other little things make this a really neat game.

Following BGG advice, I left my tactics cards in shrink; I have no intention of ever using them. Why mess with a game this good?

RATING: 9
5 / 5 - Great

I was surprised by how much I liked this. It has overtones of Lost Cities but is much, much more interesting to play. You've got tough choices to make about whether to use your cards in difficult to fill but tough high-value formations or easier low-value formations. There's also a placement and timing element which is fascinating - when and where do you start to commit your formations of varying power? Quite possibly the best short game I've ever played. See my review for more details: http://fortressat.com/index.php?option=com_content&view=article&id=2508

December 2012:  Rating of 9: Excellent game.  Very easy to teach, clean and simple mechanics. Quick to play, great for couples and a modern classic.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Solid two-player card game that involves playing a series of three-card "poker" hands.  Each three-card hand represents a battle.  To win, you must beat your opponent in five of nine battles or in three consecutive adjacent battles.

One of the interesting aspects of this game is that you may use deduction to prove that one of your completed hands cannot be beaten by your opponent.  If you can prove such, you may claim victory in the battle immediately.  This is important to do because of the existence of tactics cards.

Tactics cards are played in the same fashion as regular cards, with the limitation that you may only play one such card more than your opponent.  Some tactics cards are wild cards, some allow you to move/swap/shift cards, while others change the requirements for a particular battle.  For example, one of the cards makes it so that you must play four cards rather than three.  Hence, if you fail to properly claim victory in a battle and your opponent plays this card, you may no longer claim victory.

RATING: 7.5
Game in a Nutshell: Compete individually for each of nine lined-up flags by playing three cards at each one. The best three cards wins the given flag. Take five of the nine flags or three in a row to win.

Played as: Two-player only, with Tactic cards as well as without.

Pros: Knizia has a way of making 'discard and draw' games so interesting-- T&E, Lost Cities, even Ingenious, and now this. Always so simple, but never an easy choice among many possibilities on any given turn. It can be played a little more friendly by leaving out the tactic cards, but I feel with Tactic cards using the advanced rules is where this game shines its most. That way there's still tension even when you have the card you need because it gives your opponent the chance to counter. There's no knowing what they'll have and it really cuts down those inevitable 'I've got this in the bag' moments.

Cons: I kinda felt ripped off when I opened the box and saw how little I got for my money (nine markers, and a couple decks of cards). The luck to time-of-play ratio might be a hair high on the luck tolerance scale (this can vary depending on players, see: AP prone), though I wouldn't call this a completely luck dominated game. Can make for some sour losses (for a lighter game like this) when a tactic card crashes your party.

Other: Less luck-based then Lost Cities and maybe not the perfect replacement I originally thought. Has more direct screwage with potential to offend the more light-hearted Lost Cities players. The flip-side is the screwage is a big plus for everyone else. I've been getting a lot more play of this with a home-built version I scrapped together on the road sans Tactic cards but would probably rate the game a 6 or so under that format. I have it rated where it is assuming tactic cards and using the advanced rules.

Best Use: A quick and easy game that works nicely with new players at home or on the road.

RATING: 8
[url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/419542/big-bulleted-battle-blueprint-please-add-your-own]My Battle Line Strategy Article[/url]

After 9 plays. Battle Line is often compared to Lost Cities.  To my mind, Battle Line is a way better game.  There are several reasons.  First is the win condition.  Lost Cities' win condition forces a player to constantly evaluate a stupid algebraic expression in her head throughout the game just to get a read on where she stands in relation to her opponent.  And then, when the game is over, it's not immediately obvious who won, but you to do more algebra to find out.  It's clunky and it pushes players away from the "flow" state.  Battle Line has a very simple, visual win condition, that avoids the clunk. It also has a much greater bluffing element than Lost Cities, which this bluffaholic loves. A final difference is that, whereas in Lost Cities, you can discard on your turn and thus avoid having to play a card that will hurt you, Battle Line offers no such option.  This is a key to the insane tension that a game of Battle Line creates.

What Battle Line shares with Lost Cities is the fact that you have to commit to certain courses of action before you know whether you'll be able to get to the resources to make those commitments worthwhile.  This is another good source of tension. From a more meta-game perspective, intuition can triumph regularly in both games.  The above notwithstanding, with experience, the *feel* of play is totally different between the two games.

[EDIT]  After 18 plays, this is moving up from my original 7 rating. This has replaced LOTR:C as my favorite non-abstract game.  I spent a whole day at work trying to get this game out of my head.  This is the first time in a very long time that a game has grabbed me like this.  Mind-boggling number of factors to consider during a game.  The more I play this, the more it resembles nothing else.  the fact that a small card game with dinky components can make it right up to near the top of the rankings on a website full of component-whores suggest others feel the same way.

[EDIT]  After 35 plays, my 9 rating holds steady, but the way that I see the game continues to change.  At the moment I'm going through a period of confusion.  Despite many games and much thinking, I still feel I'm missing some fundamental insight.  It's extremely difficult to play or think about analytically, because all the probabilities that one cares about in the game are completely tangled up with one another.  Battle Line is a wall of inscrutability, but it remains maniacally fun.  And it's just a deck of cards!  It's sort of like the "Go" of card games.

[EDIT] I'm somewhere north of 200 plays now but I've lost count. This game is officially in my Pantheon of greats. An endless, sublime challenge.  A lifetime game. I've played it tons both with and without the tactics cards, and I've come to prefer it without. Two reasons: first, the tactics cards increase the randomness of the game and in doing so destroy some of the most interesting aspects of planning and resource management. There's more room for clever, skilled play without the tactics cards. Second, the tactics cards make straight flushes more important and the other kinds of formations less important, which reduces variety in play. For example, yesterday I won a game without getting a single straight flush; that could never happen if I were playing with tactics cards.

It's exceptionally hard to make truly great "small" games. Battle Line is small in every way except for the experience of playing it. For that reason it's impressed me as much as any Knizia game has (and many have)

RATING: N/A
"Schotten Totten ( Battle Line) is so very wonderful. The new Iello version is spectacular and definitive. Everything from the previous versions with the classic scenario returned and packaged for the first time in great art.

Fight for dominance on each area by playing a card in one of the areas and pulling a new card for your next turn. Reach player can only place up to three cards in each area. And the winner is determined by the poker hands you create with just those three cards.

Just a delight. I like it a lot more than Lost Cities, although the Lost Cities art is wonderful. Schotten had more depth."

RATING: 8
Play cards in combinations that are superior to your opponent's.

Plays: More than 100?
Type: Area control game = a game where you compete for control of areas.
Complexity: Light strategy game.
# of players: 2.
Playing time: 15 mins.
Player interaction: Competition for scarce resources - pawns.
Randomness: Low.

Replayability: Very high. This is one those rare "simple yet deep" games. Only takes 15 mins to play and is portable. Most of my plays have been done in cafés or pubs. Often you play best of three or best of five.
Fun: The fun lies in the decision making of hand and board management.
Memorability: Not really.

Thematic integration: Weak.
Components: Functional.

Who will like this: This is probably the best quick strategy game for 2. Cheap, quick, portable - a must have in every game collection.

RATING: 7
Interesting game...Feels like playing Lost Cities, Poker, and crunching out a logic puzzle all at the same time.  The simple rules make it seem like a walk in the park...but trust me, Battle Line is much more challenging than it appears at first glance.  Game play is centered around players, in turn, adding to a group of cards which "faces off" against an opponent's group in various "battles".  Based on various orders of color, numbers, and numerical straights, each combination lies somewhere on the hierarchy of worth.  Highest group takes that particular battle...if they can prove it!

RATING: 7
I seldom get to play 2 player games but Battle Line is portable and so elegant that I couldn't resist it. Take turns to play cards to one of nine flags and claim a flag by having a better card combination than your opponent. The first turns are deceptivingly calm but eventually you have to decide whether to complete an OK combination or wait for a better card that may never come. A true "just one more time" game.

RATING: 9
Enjoy this game very much, although we tend to play it as Schotten-Totten (cards only up to number 9, 6 cards each).  We have played with the tactics cards and, in fact, view it as two different games.  S-T is taughter and more tense, while Battle Line proper opens up the possibilities and allows for a greater fluidity of play.

RATING: 8
Like Lost Cities, but with more flags, so there's more room to set up a strategy over several turns.  In Lost Cities you often have to go charging into a suit and hope it works out, whereas here you can spread out a bit more and take your time.  Also, the tactics cards provide nifty ways out of a pinch.  They introduce a luck factor depending on which tactics you draw, but they cancel out luck at the same time by letting you substitute for cards you didn't draw, or that you played too early elsewhere.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Battle Line holds up well as a semi-mindless activity. As an interesting game, however, it's not nearly enough for me to understand the general enthusiasm towards it. First and foremost the early game is really chaotic, in the sense that a reasonable level of probabilistic reasoning can't be driven from the opening hand (or even the first 15 plays). Such an incertitude is unfortunately only toned down as turns pass and you're mostly spending the game formulating hopeful wishes, sometimes holding information about your opponent's chances on a given line without being able to do anything clever with it. As far as I went, three-of-a-kind and royal flushes are all there is, because you have little incentive to dump unwanted cards on a line and allow lower combinations to be useful to your opponent.

As a general matter of fact, you always feel at the mercy of the draw and are longing for some interesting decisions. Perhaps I'd start to see their existence after a dozen games of utter boredom, but unless I'm shown a convincingly documented sample of the ingenious intricacies of high level play, this doesn't look like a gambit worth taking. On the bright side, since everybody seems to agree that Battle Line is leagues above Lost Cities, I don't have to consider checking that one out anymore.

RATING: 8
Classic Knizia in that this is more like a mathematical puzzle than a game.  I like the analogy of Bach to Knizia (http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/150870/knizia-bach), and Battle Line certainly holds true of this analogy.

You've essentially got 6 suits of 10 numbers each.  You've got a hand of 7 cards, and 9 positions (flags) to which you can play cards.  At each position you are trying to create a "straight flush," 3-of-a-kind, all of one color, etc.  Your opponent is trying to do the same.  First person to win three contiguous positions or five total positions claims victory.

First off, you can tell that a mathematician designed this game, and it carries all of Knizia's traits of colors and numbers weakly themed to a particular narrative.  When it works, you've got great games with depth and longevity, like Ra and Lost Cities.  When it doesn't, you've got Lord of the Rings, Kingdoms, Poison, etc.  Not bad games, mind you, just games where the mathematical exercise gets a little tiresome relative to the theme.  

Battle Line is one of Knizia's games that works.  Again, it's really only a card game, and if you've never cared for card games, I don't see how you'd like this game.  However, the balance of the game is sooo tight.  It's HARD to definitively capture a flag, and practically every turn feels like a difficult choice.  Let's say you've played three "4"s to a location, and your opponent has played a "2." You haven't won the position until you can prove that they can't get the 1 and 3 of the same color as the "2," (any straight flush trumps three-of-a kind).  If you happen to have the 1 or 3 in your hand, maybe you'll want to play it in order to satisfy this "proof."  But you can't just put it anywhere, because that might enable your opponent to capture *that* particular position.  

In addition the game includes a small number of "tactics" cards.  After playing a card, a player can either draw from the "strategy" deck (number/suits) or the "tactics" deck.  The latter basically introduce modifiers / actions.  So for example, play the "traitor" card and you can take one card from your opponents side and move it to your side. Play the "mud" card and a position will be decided by four cards instead of the normal three. There are not too many of these cards, which I appreciate, and their effects are narrowly focused.  Also, given that the strategy cards (number/suits) are what win the game, deciding to forgo a draw of such a card for a tactics card can be a bit risky, which again, gets at the superb balance of this game.

Highly recommend.  Can be played at a bar.  Very simply rules that produce a sense of difficult choices and some puzzle-like deduction. Absolutely won't last more than an hour.

RATING: 8
For a game composed of very basic components with easy to learn rules, Battle Line involves some serious strategy. Decisions are essentially based on one's ability to card count, or lack thereof.

I'm glad that they changed the name to Battle Line when the game was reimplemented, because Schotten-Totten is a stupid sounding name. I can persuade people to play BL, but Schotten-Totten would have been a really tough sell.

The worst part of BL is the cards. They are so stiff that they make shuffling actually painful.

My favorite Reiner Knizia game to date.

RATING: 10
[b]Battle Line[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (10):[/COLOR]

[i]This has become one of my favorite 2p card games, especially since I play it with my wife and son (haven't taught it to my older daughter yet). Each game is extremely tense and always comes down to the wire, no matter how hopeless it may appear at certain points in the game. 

The tactics card add the perfect amount of unpredictability, but you have to be careful when playing them since they don't come into play when figuring out whether or not a flag is won.

I also can't stress how portable this game is, you can easily play it at a pub or cafe (which my wife and I do frequently). On top of that it plays quick and decisions are difficult right from the beginning. There is absolutely no wasted turns in this game, each card you play is extremely important.

This is definitely going to be an evergreen in my collection![/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]- Another great 2p game that plays quick but offers lots of strategy.
- Interesting take on basic poker mechanics being used to gain a majority of flags (scoring markers) on the table (5 or 3 that are all next to each other.)
- Tactics cards: help add an amount of uncertainty to the game; it becomes harder to make sure that you really have a flag scored. They let you manipulate the cards you have played in some interesting ways, but they are well balanced because you can only play one more tactics card than your opponent has played.[/i]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
I'm very impressed by the strategic possibilities. As others have noted, the theme is somewhat tacked on, yet at the same time I do get the feel of battle, as one card play can end a battle and break up a potential string of 3 adjacent wins, akin to a center collapsing. 

I prefer to play without the tactics cards, although I've read comments by others that they prefer to play with them. (Curiously, I like using all such cards with Knizia's somewhat similar "Ivanhoe," but not this.) 

However, it reminds me most of "Lost Cities," though I'd give it a slight edge for the battle theme (over archaeology) and the simplicity of what feels like a traditional card game. 

I've played it with a non-gamer friend--who is on record as saying not only does he not like board games, but 'hates' them--who liked it. And, my sister, a total gamer, played this in July 2013 and rated it a "9". If you want to get a non-gamer into playing this, tell them if they like poker they should give this a try.

RATING: 7
牌庫: 6色 1~10
心得:
好入門的兩人遊戲
第一個入手的新桌遊
規則:
1.贏連續三個軍旗，或總共五個就獲勝
2.牌六種顏色 1~10
3.起始七張手牌，打一張抽一張
4.出牌只能出於未分勝負的戰列
5.同花順>三條>同花>順>亂陣，相同陣比總合，全部相同則先打得贏
6.有三張如果可以證明一定贏就可以拿棋，資訊只能靠公開資訊，不可以靠手牌
7.特殊功能牌只能出比對方多一張

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a two-player tug-of-war-style card game in which players are trying to play various card combinations to a number of columns.  Cards are played one at a time, so you can see what kind of combinations your opponent is trying to make and try to outdo them (or decide it's not worth it).  You're limited by the cards you draw, so you might not actually be able to put out the optimum combo.  Additionally, each card is unique, so you can be on the lookout for specific cards, and figure out whether they've already been played, or you might draw them in the future.  There's a surprising amount going on in this simple game, and I really enjoyed it.

(1 play)

2 player

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is such a well designed and thought provoking game. Very few rules but each card you play is incredibly tense and you always feel like you're the one making the lead. You can't give too much information away as to what you're playing and once an opponent does, you can capitalize on it.

The artwork is fine and the theme makes sense although it's not incredibly strong for either. The card quality is typical GMT, stiffer cardboard that really can't take a riffle shuffle.

I usually play with the tactics cards, but sometimes when I don't want that additional level of depth, I just leave them out. The game still feels very deep even without them.

RATING: 7
It's almost like salary negotiations: whoever declares a number first loses unless they hold all the right cards. The tension is created by having to form a new line and not knowing the best card to lead. There is the element of foiled plans when you realize your opponent has already played one of the cards you needed. There is the agony of waiting to draw that last card you need, hoping your opponent isn't hoarding it away in his hand.

Quick. Easy. Strategic. It is Lost Cities and Poker combined into one game.

RATING: 6.5
Graciosillo juego de cartas que intenta simular un frente de batalla entre dos ejércitos de la antigüedad: los macedonios de Alejandro Magno y los Persas del petardo de Darío. El juego consiste en ir agrupando cartas iguales o correlativas en los diferentes puntos del frente de batalla que superen a las que ha agrupado el contrario. Las ilustraciones de las cartas dejan bastante que desear, no así la calidad de las mismas, que son de un material como plastificado bastante bueno. Para pasar un ratillo cuando no se juegue al de los Pingüinos ni al de los trenes esos del Cairo.

RATING: 10
What a great, easy to play, yet exciting little gem this is. Ultra portable, deep and engrossing. I had the good fortune to find a different version of Battle Line. With different I mean the artwork. The game is just good old Battle Line by Knizia.

I live in Hong Kong (but am Dutch) and recently I discovered a great boardgame shop here. Anyway I picked up the Chinese version of Battle Line. Which is, IMHO, superior in quality and overall look compared with the regular release.

Its comes in a very well produced sturdy box (size; two decks of cards next to eachother) with an illustration of two Chinese general of the cover.

Inside are two compartments, one for the main deck and one of the special cards and flags. Flags, this time there are REAL miniature flags, and not some generic pawns. The little flags are very well made. It are Chinese style war flags made of pewter. The cards itself are of a very good quality (better than the original) and have very nice illustrations on it... again, sorry to say, better than the original. (and no, I am not the producer of salesman of this version) I just am very enthousiastic about this version. Its sleek, better produced and more affordable. Slightly more than half of the original price. (approx. 11 us$) The only "downside" is that the writings on the card are in Chinese, the rule book (nicely printed in color with illustrations) are in Chinese too. But even though I can't read Chinese, I don't care, since the rules are exactly the same as the original.

To cut it short, I highly recommend this neatly produced, great looking little gem! You can find it here: http://wargames.com.hk/oscommerce/product_info.php?products_id=3581

RATING: 8
This is a 2 player only game, designed by Reiner Knizia.

Gameplay consists of two opponents who face off across a 'battle line' and attempt to win the battle by taking 5 of 9 flags or 3 adjacent flags. Winning a flag is decided by placing cards into 3 card poker-type hands on either side of the flag (similar to straight flush, 3 of a kind, straight, etc). The side with the highest 'formation' of cards wins the flag.

RATING: N/A
Don't come near me with the Tactics cards. They take a great game of managing/balancing (up to) 9 simultaneous poker hands and add an element of "look what I found!" that just creates this artificial sense of drama, this insistence that there always needs to be a dramatic Hail Mary available to the player in second place. It's too damn gamey; just let the better player be better -- it's OK. GMT has such a raging hard-on for unpredictability and "gotcha!" moments and for me it kills this game. You're already managing unpredictability and imperfect information because it's a card game; having to deal with asymmetrical unevenly-balanced special power cards on top of that is just piling it on. This is an excellent traditional-feeling card game without the Tactics cards.

RATING: 10
Our favorite "we've only got 30 minutes before bed" game. We replaced the pawn shaped flags with colorful wooden buttons from a toy store and stored the game in a small deck box. Surprising amount of strategy for such a simple game. I've taught it to a bunch of people now and I always tell them "about half way through the game you're going to have an 'a hah!' moment". Never fails.

Variant #1: We usually play without the tactics cards, but recently we tried this variant and liked it. Remove one of the tactics cards and place the other nine under the flags. Whenever a player claims a flag, the OTHER player gets the tactics card that was under it. This does not count against your card limit (i.e. you may still have seven regular cards no matter how many tactics cards you've acquired).

Variant #1a: As in #1 but the tactics card must be played on the next turn or is discarded face up. Discarding does not count as a turn.

Updated to a 10 as the game just gets more fun the more we play it.

I backed "Solitaire for Two" on kickstarter mostly so I could have a set of tiles to play this on a park bench on a windy day. Also picked up Rummikub for the same reason, but I'm still working out the best way to modify the Rummikub tiles to support six suits.

RATING: 6
Okay card game, but nothing special. Battle cards are hoplites, chariots, elephants, etc in five colors and with numbers from 1-10. Also a small deck of tactics cards that have various effects. Opposing players place cards one at a time to build "formations" (really three-card poker hands like three-of-a-kind, flush, etc). Attempt to defeat opponent's "formations" and capture flags set between the players. First player to capture three adjacent flags or five flags overall wins. A decent filler for two people.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[b]RO[/b]: [i]10/17/2015[/i]

Another entry into the series I like to call Reiner Knizia's: Math.

Jokes aside Battle Line is an outstanding two-player card game where you're trying to make "formations" out of cards in certain number/color/suit sequences which have varying strength based on the probability of you actually getting the cards to make it. Additionally it brings concepts of positioning into play as between each stack of cards representing a formation is a "flag" when a number of cards are placed your formation is complete and if your formation beats your opponent's formation (or you can show that based on the cards already visible on the table it is impossible for your opponent to to beat you) you take the flag. Add to this interesting and occasionally brain burning dimension tactics cards which can really mix things up and you have a very interesting, easy to learn and play little game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.4
Players: 2
Time: 30 min.

Nine stones are set in a line between the two players. Players alternate playing cards one at a time on their side of the stones, creating sets of 3 cards at each stone. The objective is to win either 5 of the 9 stones, or 3 stones in a row. Like poker, rarer card sets beat more common card sets. The card deck consists of 6 colors with denominations 1-10.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.1
This is a two player card game by Reiner Knizia (and it is very Knizia - similar in a lot of ways to Lost Cities). Players draft cards one at a time from their hands to nine positions. They are trying to make sets of cards, in order of value from Hordes (no set), Flush, Straight, Three of a Kind, Straight Flush. These sets capture flags. The game can be won in two ways. A dramatic victory in which one player breaksthrough and captures three adjacent flags, or a slow war of attrition resulting in an envelopment where a player captures a majority of the flags.
The game gets increasingly tense as players choices for where to play cards diminish.

RATING: 7
The best of Knizia's many "light" card games. (OK, that's just my opinion, and I certainly haven't come close to playing them all - but it's by FAR the best of the many I've played.) This feels similar in many ways to Lost Cities (despite being quite different in what you're trying to do), but offering a much greater variety of options. A classic example of a game with simple rules offering great tension in play, while tending to play differently every time (which is where Lost Cities falls down). My wife and I actually haven't played this since 2012 (I think, perhaps it was early 2013) and I'm unlikely to request it in the near future, as these days I prefer something a bit "meatier". But that in no way detracts from this game, which is excellent for what it is.

RATING: 10
BP
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
What kind of a genius is Knizia? I mean, come the hell on! Where does this shit come from? He just reaches into the aether, rubs three rules together, and then this piece of goddamn GOLD falls out of the ether. This game has the same kind of classical perfection that, I don't know, like Go and Hold 'Em have. 

The tension, the way you start by probing for information, trying not to commit, trying to hold back, until you absolutely can't any more, then commit... the betting, the play of the possibilities in the opportunity space... this game is ETCHED IN SILVER AND GOLD.

Also: it's thematic. The Schotten-Totten theme didn't fit, but turning it into an ancient battle theme weirdly works... mostly because of how much of the tension is waiting for commitment, forcing yourself to commit, probing for information, etc.

NOTE: I've only played this without the special power cards, a la the original design.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
----------

Great, tactical filler.  It's fun, and it packs some tense decisions in a very short play time.

Tactics cards also breathe fresh life into the game and make it a bit more chaotic, which I think is a good thing.  I also like the strategy involved in the timing of tactics cards.  For example, if my wife has one more tactic card down than me and one more in her hand, I know she can't play that next card until I lay down a tactic card of my own.. so by stalling, I can make her waste a precious inventory slot in her hand.  Some people don't like the Tactics cards, but this is why I prefer BL over Schotten Totten.


This is my #2 filler, and I think everyone should have a copy in their collection.  And, if you don't want to buy the game, you can print kilgore's retheme for free.  If you are torn between BL and Lost Cities, I would DEFINITELY advise you to get BL, which is much more cerebral than Lost Cities (which can pretty much be played on autoplay).

RATING: 8
Fun game! Nine flags make up the battle line. The winner is the first to breach the line (by claiming three adjacent flags) or envelop the line (by claiming any five). Players take turns making troop card "formations" behind their side of each flag. Formations are essentially three card poker hands, with a wedge (straight flush) beating a phalanx (three of kind) and so forth. A small number of tactics cards give the game just enough variety to break inflexible strategies. Normally a flag is claimed when each player has complete formations on their sides, but I love that you can force your opponent to surrender a flag if you can prove their formation will be inferior when it's finished. The girlfriend didn't like this game, although she didn't hate it. As much as I like the tactics cards, maybe next time I'll show her Battle Line's cousin Schotten Totten instead and see if that's a bigger hit. See Schotten Totten for a detailed comparison between it and Lost Cities.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game's rating has slowly climbed from a 6 to a 9.5 over a one year period. At first I found the game only mildly interesting but with each subsequent game I've grown to appreciate the near constant tension this simple game can create. The requirement to play a card at the beginning of your turn often conflicts with your need to limit the courses of action to which you'll become commited. And the need to guard against a breakthrough victory adds a whole other level of concern to the game. And, finally, the ability to claim a flag by proving your opponent cannot prevail, thus closing down their formation and potentially squeezing them into a position where the only good card to play is no cards at all - but you must play a card! - is delicious. Having all the cards you need in hand for a given flag provides a welcome breather as you can throttle down the tension over the next few turns and pray that the cards you draw will also help keep the pressure off. Sadly, these breaks seldom last long. But, then, that's also a good thing.

The game is easy to learn and easy to teach and has gone over well with both gamers and non-gamers to whom I've introduced it. So, to my mind, it serves well as a two-player gateway game that both I and the new gamer can enjoy. The production quality is about the only thing that bothers me. I prefer the theme of this version (seriously - Scotsmen battling over the placement of pasture boundary stones?) and the art is fine but the cards are cheap, thin, and untextured and the edges show wear almost immediately. Since I expect to put this game to heavy use, having the main component begin to break down from the first playing is disappointing. Especially considering that despite its being only a simple card game its price is fairly high and as such discourages keeping multiple replacement copies on hand. With that in mind, I've invested in a couple of copies of Schotten Totten (with the tactics cards). The card quality is noticably improved and the cost is less but I do miss the military theme and especially the elephant (10's) cards... 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Schotten Totten is better, so just take the 10s out when you play this. I like Battle Line's theme and pawns more.

Gets a little routine after too many plays, especially if it's against the same opponent.

RATING: 8
[29 plays] Excellent little 2 player card game. The GMT cards are nice looking, but a little thin. However, they will hold up just fine if treated nicely. Mine still look brand new.

Many comments say that this is "just like" Lost Cities. Perhaps emotionally I can see the comparisons , as the alternating card play gets quite tense as the game progresses, but otherwise I think the comparison is overstated. The flag capture mechanic adds a very pleasant angst to the contest, and I also enjoy how it eliminates the need for mathematical calculation to determine the game winner.

This is a good game for alomst anyone's collection. (yes, even if you own Lost Cities)

Most recent play Feb '14.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Battle Line is my favorite 2-player card game. In this game, players compete for Flags by playing down cards from their hand in sets of three (albeit one card at a time). There are six suits (colors) of cards, with values running from 1-10. Each of the 9 Flags is available for competition. Some sets beat other sets, allowing you to claim a Flag. Winning requires a breach (claiming 3 adjacent Flags) or an envelopment (claiming any 5 Flags). Special Tactics cards break specific rules of the game, adding a strong element of the unknown. You can remove the Tactics cards to play Schotten-Totten, and added bonus.

I just love the blend of analytical thinking and chance in this game. To win, you need to keep track of what's going on and remain flexible. If you become too attached to winning a particular Flag, it can spell your doom. Knizia's Battle Line is heavier than his LotR: the Confrontation, but just light *enough* to play fairly briskly. I definitely prefer Battle Line to Lost Cities, which is also a good game, perhaps because Battle Line feels a little less "mathy". Every game has been fun, and more strategies have become apparent with each play. This game truly gives you many interesting/desirable options, and then makes you choose only one - based upon an analysis of the playing field, your own resources, and your grasp of opponent psychology.

Battle Line also plays fast enough we fall prey to the "just one more game" syndrome - which can be a curse if you have to get up early the next morning! [To my chagrin - just a little :) - my wife beats me at this game most of the time, and we maintain a pretty intense rivalry.]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
This is an abstract take on a trick-taking game.  The game is won by collecting a certain number of flags that are set up in the middle of the table.  These are collected by playing cards of different numbers and colors on your side of those flags.  Once both players have a set of three cards on either side of a flag the player with the highest set based on a hierarchy very similar to poker rules (a flush of the same color beats a straight run which beats 3 of a kind which beats high value and of course a "straight flush" beats everything).

Abstract and trick-taking games aren't my thing and for a quick two-player activity I'd much rather play something else.  It is made a little bit more interesting by the special ability cards and I like the idea that you can never play more than 1 more than your opponent played so by never playing a special ability card you limit your opponent to playing only one during the game (and if they've drawn more than one they've just wasted those draws).  This still isn't enough to make it very interesting to me though.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.3
Great filler game to introduce non-gamers to board games.
Plays in 10-15 minutes. Lends itself to easy teaching as it is based on several basic poker hands (you create 3 card hands).  Such an elegant game with some deduction as more and more cards are laid out, to see how high your chances are of pulling off stronger poker sets to beat opponent. There's always tension as you try to reveal what poker set you're starting as slowly as possible but your hand size forces you to lay out cards and commit to a poker set usually earlier than you'd like to commit to it.
A "Classic" quick strategy game!

RATING: 7
[GMT 2004 edition, 1st printing] {Ancient; set collection, utility cards, drafting} Battle Line is a tactical 2p card game with moderate confrontation. Since it's quite confrontational, my wife doesn't like it much.

The theme of Battle Line is just pasted on. Quality of the cards is very disappointing: too thin to be durable. In addition, the artwork is quite horrible! 

Luck in card draw may marginally affect the result of a play; I believe skillful card playing is more crucial to winning. Without a good plan and being ignorant of the opponent's strategy, one can hardly win this game. If a player pays attention to the details, it's not difficult to guess the cards in opponent's hand (in particular the number cards).

'Proving you win' is the core of Battle Line. This encourages players compete aggressively for the flags. If you claim a flag before your opponent places all 3 cards, you will make him have 1 fewer place to discard useless cards and he cannot "buy time" to wait for a better card in the draw. Another good rule is the special winning condition, which forces players decide carefully which battle field to give or take.

[Comparison] Compared to Schotten Totten, Battle Line is more dynamic with the tactic cards. The one card threshold is a nice rule to thwart a player from hoarding and abusing the tactic cards; without this rule, Battle Line may become too chaotic to control. It's usually a good tactic to use the tactic cards late so that the opponent is forced to withhold the tactic cards until you move. This provides you more flexibility and limits possible move of your opponent. With wise moves, tactic cards can destroy a sure-win proof dramatically.

[Comparison] I prefer Battle Line to Lost Cities because it, with the tactic cards, isn't just a pure calculation game and it is less luck-driven. The tactic cards provide many tactical options, the 'proving you win' rule creates additional excitement and the special winning condition makes the game more fun. Nonetheless, I like both games as they are fun and tactical.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[u]Key Words:[/u] Ancient Warfare - Card Game - 2-Player - 30mins

[u]Summarized Opinion:[/u] Interesting, short card game that feels like a cross between Poker and Lost Cities.

[u]My Rating Explained:[/u] Good game, will gladly play.

[u]Verbose Assessment:[/u]

This game took a while to grow on me. Bought it a long time ago, played it a few times back then but it didn't grab me. It spent years on the shelf.

Then I tried it again, and after the first difficult game (what's this formation again?...) it started to come together. There's an interesting game here.

It's pretty short, but requires some thought and good play to win. The main thing is not to commit too early and pay attention what cards have already come out. The more information you have, the better you can play your cards. Sometimes it's better to just give up some location rather than take a risk on a promising or important one.

I'm still undecided on the tactics cards - it seems that who-ever takes the most ends up winning the game. Needs some more plays to decide.

If you like Lost Cities but want to try something more challenging, you owe it to yourself to try Battle Line.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Purchased at 2007 WBC

EDIT 11/20/2014
After playing a bunch of Battle Line with my mom this week I have come to the conclusion that this is one of my all-time favorites.  It's a perfect game for 2-players.  I don't think anyone has ever done so much with so little.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A worthy elaboration on the mechanics presented in Lost Cities. Players are able to make sets not just of similarly colored cards, but a range of three-card poker hands. The extra options adds a deduction element and increases the complexity of choosing when and where to "invest" your cards. As in Lost Cities, there are restrictions on when and where you can place a card, so timing is key, as is logic. Figuring out what your opponent is likely to be holding allows you to form combinations just good enough to win. Overplaying, or attempting to overplay a particular column more often than not gets you into trouble. In the end, it's a different animal from Lost Cities, but the family resemblance is certainly there.

The Tactics Cards are largely unnecessary, but not without their charm. That the rules restrict their play means that you can effectively choose to play without them even when you haven't outright removed them from the game.

And of course I'm happy to see that the game is being re-released under its original name: [thing=372][/thing].

RATING: 9.5
Hands down, my favorite card game filler. There's so much strategy and tension packed into this two-player battle. It's one of the games in which my husband and I are pretty evenly matched, so each play feels very exciting. The tactics cards add a lot; there's some decision-making involved in playing one because, while you get a definite advantage from it, you also give your opponent the ability to play one of their own. Also, Battle Line has a great mechanism for declaring victory over a flag--it's not just the first person to lay a winning hand, but rather the person who can prove their hand is superior to any their opponent might play. It's a small detail that really adds a lot to the gameplay.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In my opinion, the special-power cards  don't add much to the basic game engine underlying this game (i.e. - the elegant, simple and streamlined design found Battle Line's predecessor, Schotten-Totten), and in fact bog it down unnecessarily. But then, this packaging of the game allows players to remove the special cards and play with just the original design (which I recommend). The theme and artwork here are far more realistic and "serious" than the light, colorful, silly and cartoonish precept and presentation of Schotten-Totten (which I prefer), but gameplay sans special cards is the same. With or without the special-power cards, Battle Line is a fantastic two-player game - among my favorites.

RATING: 10
This game gets played more than any other in my collection right now. My fiance can't get enough of it. She never wants to play anything else! The rules are simple, but the flow of play creates several diverse scenarios of mini-strategies every time we play. I love the decisions in this game. And the tactic cards are perfectly designed for some unique twists. My rating started out somewhere around a 7. Over time, it has climbed and never dropped. Get this game, especially if you have a lot of 2-player opportunities.

For me, the idea of "talking my girl into playing a game" became something of the past after I had Battle Line. Now, my new challenge is "talking my girl into playing a NEW game."

The more I game, the more I realize it's important for a great game to have a well-integrated theme into the game play. Battle Line is still the best in its class, so it still get a high rating, but I'm lowering out of the 9s and locking it into a solid 8 for now.

Update 11/19/2012 - I've been playing it on my iPad a lot lately and had a few sessions against my wife again. It's just so tense that when I checked my rating on it, I felt it deserved a bump. Back up to an 8.5!

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 5
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 3
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 1
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 5


Overall - 4

RATING: 8
Can you make a compelling war game that really captures something meaningful about ancient linear combat using nothing more than a slightly modified deck of playing cards and some pawns? Yes. I think you can. But Reiner Knizia actually DID.

This is a subtle game of deception and abstract maneuvers built atop a foundation of chance, probability and the unknown.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
While I happen to enjoy this,  I would have never predicted it's consistently high ratings,  given the game's design.  In essence,  it is three card poker in seven different hands.  I am a big poker fan, so it's appeal makes sense to me,  however,  I am somewhat perplexed by it's widespread popularity.  That being said,  I would play this game at any time.

Concerning this edition of the game - Battle Line - I am not enamored with the card quality,  graphic design and overall presentation of the game.  To me,  Schotten-Totten is a much preferred edition,  as the bright colored graphics and large numbers are far more functional and actually fit the theme of this game much better.  After all,  it is a light game of head to head poker comparisons,  not a re-creation of a deep tactical war.  

Once the most recent release of Schotten-Totten included the tactics cards, this was relegated to the trade pile.

Letter Grade: A.  Upgrades the core mechanics of Lost Cities, an already excellent game. [05-08-09]

Simply brilliant.  Feels like a thorough upgrade from Lost Cities; instead of having to manage the tense timing of each suit of cards, one has the added complexity of each suit having a direct effect on all the others. RATING: 9
For such a simple looking game Battle Line has an awful lot going on. Similar to its spiritual ancestor Lost Cities, each time you lay a card feels either fantastic or disastrous. Tactics card, however, can change any fight mid-battle.

The game is lightning fast to set up, simple to teach, and reliably plays in a half hour. It's amazing how many tough decisions and interesting strategies can be packed into such a tiny box and playtime.

Battle Line is worth the hype.

RATING: 8
A little, fast playing gem that is extremelly engaging.

This game combines perfectly tough decisions and very fast flow of play. While most games belong to only one of these seemingly opposite spheres, mr knizia delivers with extreme simplicity, yet rewarding depth. It is a most enjoyable game, one that makes me want to play it again and again even for the whole evening. (which says a lot for what is basically a filler)

On the negative side, the theme, though well made for an abstract (well done gmt!) is not as attractive to everyone, and even though I personally love it, I can understand that several players are uninterested in the war theme.

This game has really touched a soft spot on me.

RATING: 8
I have never had a game toy with my affections as much as you battle line.  You tease me with 2 card straight flushes, and tempt me with three of a kind.  The second I turn my eyes away from the straight flush, the card draw punishes me for my lack of faith and commitment.  Why oh why?

Seriously,  this is one fantastic card game.  Brinksmanship, handmanagement, and a bit of double think all tied into this card game.  Intuitive, addictive, fun, and cerebral.  A keeper on all accounts.

Maybe one of these days I will try out the tactics cards, but I don't see the point.  At least they are nice enough to keep them in their own wrapper so you keep them nice and fresh.  

And since everyone compares this to Lost Cities, I will too.  They are completely different.  Both warrant a spot in any collection.

RATING: 8.5
PROS: A masterpiece of light strategy, with a clever mechanic, and a lot of logic and anticipation with a pinch of poker. The Tactics cards add a great flavour to the game and I'm always tempted not play them (this way my opponent can only play one Tactics card in the entire match) but I end up using them because sometimes they are the only way to "save" some almost-lost flags. So I must conclude they're a great addition.

CONS: The box is roughly 3 times larger than the components actually require (This will always be part of my analysis since I live in Brazil and must import most of the games I buy); The components look cheap for the game price (the flags aren't even flags! Flags depicting different regions would be a great addition to theme and game value); The art and design look very raw; The cards are a strange size that is slightly larger than standard so I'm having trouble finding the appropriate sleeves for this game. Also, they could be american sized for instance, to reduce table space required. Every time I setup the game, I must first lay 9 cards side by side to measure the correct distance between flags. A small demountable bar (like in Arena: Roma II) could easily solve this and, again, add value. The rulebook is a B&W folded A4 regular paper depicting cards with the names of their colours written on them (!!!). I bought games that had WAY better components, art and design for the same price. Also some things could be better written and explained in the rules.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Oh yeah, and the randomness stick less in your eye in this one...

RATING: 7.6

Extremely confrontational, which lead to some not-so fun moments with the wife :P. The theme is simple yet relevant. Once my wife and I played this game, Lost Cities never came back to the table. Overall, a game that is much deeper than it looks, and one that can be whipped out and set up in very little time.

RATING: 7.5
Battle Line is a deceptively simple card game that plays in about 15 minutes. It is akin to playing nine separate but concurrent three card poker hands. Players vie for five "flags" (or three in a row) to win the game. The "Tactics" cards add variety and new challenges. Battle Line is easy to teach and fun to play.

RATING: 9
Great game!  Every game is full of interesting decisions and plays to completion in half an hour or so.  The rule that you can only ever play one more tactics card than your opponent is one that we overlooked for a while, but which is absolutely essential to keep the game from being dominated by the luck-of-the-draw in the tactics deck.  With that rule, this is really a good two-player game.

RATING: N/A
Battle Line is a two-player card game built around the theme of warfare during the age of Alexander the Great. Battle Line features 60 full-color Rodger MacGowan/Mark Simonitch cards depicting the prominent formations of the period (War Elephants, Heavy Cavalry, Phalanx, etc.) and 10 full-color tactics "wildcards" that give players extra flexibility and choices and help make each new battle wildly different from the last.
\13\ \13\ 
To win, you must create powerful formations along your side of the line of battle that are superior to those of your enemy. Victory goes to the player who wins 5 of the 9 battle flags (an envelopment) or three adjacent flags (a breakthrough). Based on Reiner Knizia's original design published in Germany as Shotten-Totten, Battle Line enhances and expands that game system to give players even more tactical options and gut-wrenching decisions.
\13\ \13\ 
How will you muster your battle line? Will you use your elephants to trample a hole in the enemy front, or send Alexander to the front to win a critical flag? Maybe you'll dispatch the Companion cavalry to lead or reinforce a crucial point in the line, or perhaps you"ll rely on your spies to determine the enemy"s strengths and weaknesses before committing your best forces. You'll always have plenty of choices. With every card play, you'll determine the strength and direction of your attack while plotting to fend off your enemy's advances.

RATING: 6
Two players build 9 different competing poker hands simultaneously, by alternating turns playing one card at a time. It's incredibly simple, yet strategically complex as you try to bluff your opponent into over committing at the locations of your bad hands. The theme is completely tacked on, but the slow build up and push and pull of the various hand strengths does end up looking kind of like an ancient Battleline...so well played, Dr. Knizia.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I gave this game the short shrift before because I had only played it on iOS. There's a tiny rule change there that reduces your hand size for the basic/beginner game that really reduces your flexibility and doesn't change an awful lot, so if you're trying it out on iOS, go straight to the advanced game.

Here are the basics if you haven’t played before: you have nine flags that represent lanes or columns of troops. You have a hand of seven cards. There are sixty cards of six colors/suits that are numbered 1 to 10. You’re attempting to build three card combinations that resemble poker hands: suited straights, three-of-a-kinds, straights, flushes, then “hosts” which is just a sum. At the end of your turn (or beginning if you’re playing the advanced rules) you can claim one flag. The first person to claim three adjacent flags or five non-adjacent flags wins.

This feels like a superior version of Lost Cities, a game my wife and I used to play a good bit, but I’m not sure why that is. It’s still a game of numbers, but Battle Line feels like it somewhat mimics the feel of a battle. You need to be careful about overcommitting and misinterpreting your opponent’s actions.

If there’s any cons about the game is that it is ultimately a deck of cards and some pawns. There’s not much here, but frankly the rules get out of the way of the game and it’s just more portable for being what it is. If this was Kickstarted today you could expect plastic molded flags, a playmat, and foil cards with full-bleed art.

Definitely worth the purchase.

RATING: N/A
Two player majority control card game. The battle line consists of a row of 9 flag markers. Play a card/Draw a card to build 3-card poker hands in front of each flag for control. Highest hand takes the flag, majority of flags wins.  I respect the purist view but...  For me the tactics cards are the crux of play with back-and-forth manipulation of the formations. For traveling/camping, just take the deck of cards an add nine proxy flag cards.

RATING: 7.2
The Tactics cards add some serious swing to the game, compared to [thing=372]Schotten-Totten[/thing], but I'm not sure that swing is a good thing.  It makes Battle Line feel more exciting, but the elegance of the "tactics"-free "battles" in [thing=372]Schotten-Totten[/thing] is also overshadowed.  On the other hand, rather than just numbly drawing at the end of your turn, you must make a tough decision about which deck you will trust to give you something useful; made more interesting by the spiteful uses for most of the tactics cards.  All in all, fun, with a short time requirement.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A two player only card game of ancient armies facing off in a pitched battle. Each player plays one card per turn against any one of nine objectives, the aim is to build up three card sets that beat each other like poker hand e.g. three cards all the same number (three of a kind) beats three all the same colours (a flush). Despite having very few rules the game is surprisingly tactical and difficult with a host of hard decisions on every turn.

Theme: Ancient armies
Mechanic: Set building, area control
Ideal players: 2 only
Length: Short
